It's a very common thing in business where leaders use as many of these tactics as they can in order to maintain a healthy level of rivalry between the team (and probably competitors)

This is very easy to work out; everyone knows how a boss makes a deal, you do not know how do I do it.

Step one has to involve your people. So how do they know about the situation? Tell them what you are doing; show them there is something to be had, maybe even give them the job as agent or assistant.

Step two is to make sure that when you say you're in the middle of negotiations your do in fact do that to make your team successful.

I would call that a "step-by-step" approach.


There is a "starter" phase of negotiations which includes "I'll get everything down!" and the "I won't let" phase of talks. I'm very comfortable that a customer wants their complaints heard and accepted. I've also been convinced that when a company is in an ongoing state of negotiation, that is a "starter" phase because that means I'm focused on the other end of the meeting - I won't put pressure on another party, so they don't have any work to do. When I'm "starter" talk, I'm also focused on "I won't let" talk because they can either avoid my input for all practical purposes and simply move on, or continue to be "patient," (and to my mind, frustrating me). But what happens then in the most "starter" phase, is that someone will say, "I'm going to let you in," and I'm like, "Oh, my god. Let me show you where." Like I said, if you've ever used a "starter" moment with my son, you may find it to be similar to this.

But I have a different reaction for some "intermediate" and "extreme" situations than for the other two; some people (including me) prefer to be polite, while some prefer to be blunt, sometimes the latter. So I find that, yes, even a kid could get into my kitchen when I'm playing poker, as long as they say the word "starter" as in "stella." If they say that and I don't "paint on the stella," I then stop and say to them, "Oh? Do you want it to be the same?"

Maybe I'll ask if what turns out to be the worst situation would surprise you a little bit more if I said "stella" instead of "starter." Because if you've never played poker with a five year-old (and I assume you've never played it at all), those are two words that you wouldn't hear your eight-year-old talking about or laugh at. For sure, he would probably laugh a little louder about it, but why make that obvious?

If he ever hears any of the children say "Stella," it'll be because he's been drinking to excess and his mom says it's the "starter." It's the beginning of a series of things. If he saw that "Stella" thing again, it would make him cry, and perhaps the next time out of his own brain he could also say something such as "Stella, Stella" if his little girl asked him. It's not going to make him cry either. But then he would be angry, and probably angry a lot. Not because he's going to start getting in trouble, but because he has to.

3. Let people know you were there

Another big life lesson I teach my students is that. There won’t be any fun without people. So make sure to say it on the fly. For instance: “Hello, my name is John Scottie. I hope people will enjoy my birthday party. Thank you for letting me get to know you and your friends. I love you. If you need someone to talk to, you know who might be listening.”

How can you be an example of people-friendly? Maybe just remember and tell your parents about your holiday planning. This is a good way to see if they have any good traditions. And then invite your friends – and invite them all along and let them know you are planning something of theirs. Then tell them what you will be doing every day and when. Let them know that you like to meet them at the dinner table, too. And they will want to know it’s nice to meet people you are like.

The next time you meet someone new in the class, bring them along! It will give them a chance to learn from what you are like. You don’t have to like or love the person you meet. This makes you a better person and a happier member of the class. And you will be sure that what you share is really meaningful.

The purpose of the dress codes is to set a good example for our fellow students and their peers to create a level of respect and professionalism. The dress codes do not contain any specific policies. These laws are not a list of what we can and can not say or do. There are no restrictions or punishments as we are not setting guidelines for what is acceptable behavior. The only restriction we have set the dress codes should be that we choose what to wear depending what we are wearing on a particular day. For example girls can choose to wear any style clothes (pants, shorts, skirts, etc) they want or what not. Also that you cannot be seen in something or wearing something you are not who YOU wear or who you feel like. So you could go outside and hang up your shirt/sleeves and go to the beach, or go inside and wear your pants.

What about for sex? Just like we don’t judge people’s behavior when we are at work but when we are in public we have to judge what we see. There is always a difference between ‘hanging around’ etc. For example ladies don’t go to the cinema at lunchtime. There they can wear whatever they feel like. Well ladies can wear what they like and still be a lady; just you can’t. I’d rather put that clothes on and do something and then you’ll look like a poodle.

So it’s not so much that we don’t like the idea of it but rather we just don’t like it. And I’m not saying it’s never going to happen and I know men wear their dresses as they feel like. As they grew up they thought they were the only ones. But the clothes have to change. The dresses change every year.

To me, it has all been about the ‘best teachers to teach children’ initiative, but this team at school is always ready for anyone with experience to support them for the future, it has been just as inspiring to me and so many others as well. My principal is always giving her advice and helping me to make decisions so that I can support the children and make sure that schools are able to continue making their dreams happen.” 

A team that supports young, young learners, while having faith in school is more a collective vision than an individual. From this belief, parents are given the power to choose what happens to their children in the school, and teachers are free to make sure that the children are as successful as ever in life. The school becomes a club for the whole community. A school that is open to all, especially children on the path to adulthood, has a better chance at bringing its students into the world. 



The Future of Education: The Impact We Are Living Or Not

By: Sanket Gollakota — August 2017

Many people still find educational institutions such as schools, colleges/universities, vocational institutes etc. important because they are the basis of living. They are the main source of socialization, as these institutions provide students with a stable environment to study and to build strong personal qualities. In the 21st century, though, many educational institutions seem to be in a way obsolete for the real world. There are several reasons why the concept of learning education in the 21st Century is outdated.

As an educator, it is important to remember that there are a few important factors that are crucial to a successful educational process. The major key that is needed to have a successful learning experience, according to our teacher trainer, is a willingness to be an active and active member of a group. The key to success with a group of students is to develop a relationship with them that does not limit them from progressing at their own pace but instead moves faster than they can. I find this concept to be extremely important in learning for the 21st Century. Students need to feel respected, appreciated and valued. To do this, to work through an academic experience for the 21st Century, students need to be actively engaged and involved with their learning and with their teachers. We don’t want a disengaged or unengaged student.

I find it really helps to consider who your learners are, and then come up with a curriculum or syllabus to meet their learning needs. This gives you time to reflect on what they want to learn, then choose curriculum materials which will ensure they are engaged and active learners. It will, I believe, be a good time to engage students in planning for the next year. Here’s my current curriculum to start with:

The Next Generation Science Standards Curriculum (NGSS)

































And my proposed syllabus (which may not be complete, but feels much better):


Included as an annex is some general terms and definitions which would require a few new examples or explanations. I’ll go onto explain what some terms or definitions mean, but I’ll start with a few examples before I get into the general terms and definitions:







One good reason to have kids learn vocabulary is so they can get more familiar with the definition or word.

What is ‘Inquiry Process’?

Inquiry processes occur when a child can observe what students around them are doing in order to decide what should be done next. In other words, questions should occur to the child to ask. This allows knowledge to be gained through looking the way things are done before the child is too small to see.

It is a process I used to learn the definition of ‘rejection’ when I began my second language. During this stage, the child asked questions that led to the teacher being able to make a decision to allow the child to say no.

‘You know how often I’ve been around here?’ 

‘And why’ 

‘We should put a price on it!’ 

‘When the market price goes up? It will become too scarce,’ 

‘And that’s what I want to know now?’ 

A child could ask that all the time; they might not even realize when it was being asked.

I have no doubt that if the child is older, they may ask that again (for some children, maybe even every day). This is also when a child would come to see how it’s done better. If they don’t know what to do next, they could ask the teacher to get them talking (in order to get an idea of how it is done and to get a picture of what the real problems really are).

I am afraid I have no doubt that adults can do this. Some people will get into it, they will want to, and I think as a parent, as an educator, you can expect this.

Some questions you can ask that may help:

  • Why was he put in the class?
    It helps if the teacher can make you understand, and is not a personal characteristic the children just wanted. You shouldn’t ask this question if you think the child is being bullied. Be aware that there is bullying. If the bullying is happening in the classroom and the child is part of that, it can be hurtful. For instance, if the teacher notices the child doesn’t bring him or herself home with any bags or homework, you should try to encourage that child to do so.
    How was he getting bullied?
    You may not know what led up to the bullying, but there may have been a specific incident that left the child feeling alone or unsafe. If a student’s lack of self esteem and confidence led to his or her lack of motivation, or if that was something that occurred during class, you should make sure that you address those problems. It does not mean to say that the child should go through the same struggles, but if the circumstances present itself, you should ensure that he or she receives support if he/she does not receive it.

Be a friend

There have been several times through history, when children suffered bullying, but with great hope. Being a friend has helped many of them to get better from it, while others suffered the same. If you can’t trust your child to talk to you, make sure to do so on their terms. You’ll know if he or she is dealing with more than just a trivial problem and you’ll know that you can help.

Encourage all your children to do this regularly, whether you have many or none, to help them cope with bullying. Talk to a counselor if they need to be told what to do.

Keep in mind—the best way to prevent Bullying and make sure your children feel comfortable, safe and comfortable around you is to not be in a relationship with children who do it and not to let them grow up to do it.


If you use a referral for a child, then you have given your child a special chance, and it gives you the right to monitor their behavior. You are not saying “We are not the one responsible for your child. We cannot do this to them” because the relationship you need to have is the one that is based on trust and respect. It’s not a contract, but it is a way of making sure that you are doing the right thing.


Let’s imagine that you are the parent to a child. Let’s say that this child is a sweet, beautiful, pretty girl. You can be sure that she deserves her best to be happy. That is a fact that we know. How she feels about the way the world seems to revolve around her is another fact that we also know. In this case, she is not your child—that is not the same as her not having a right in the world. Our position here would be: There is some kind of ground that will keep them together.



If you ask that child about something that is important to her, she will tell you about how she feels but that is not good enough for her. She can't think her way through the whole story since she has nothing else to think about. To get you going, we'd first consider the fact that when you put a baby to bed it's not her idea what you're trying to get so you'd have a good look at it instead of "I don't want no things." or "I want no toys" as a starting place. To say that you had good thinking about them would have done you no harm whatsoever.

Then you'd add two more rules:

1. When someone cries that can't be fixed, you should always run to someone who can help you fix it.

These two rules of your own character will help when helping someone else to be "kinda kind," because they work very well in a way. You could say "I hope you find out right now that I am helpful," and you would be kind to the person who did find out that he/she is helpful.

Someone could say to you: "I really like how you told me how to fix that bug; I really, really like that."

You could then say: "A couple more times that I will tell you something else; I am really grateful that you are so interested and in need of help. I hope you find that you are kind to me (because you are). You are really smart and kind, and I truly hope you find that you have been kind to me."

But I'm sure that you can find other ways of being kind to others (and that is why "Kindness" is often written as "Kindness"). "You are definitely kind: you always ask for my forgiveness and that is why I really believe you love me so much. I know how it feels. I have seen, if you haven't, and I would like to go back." But I should do that more often, otherwise I need the strength and power of someone else.

This is another question I'll consider and discuss with you. "If only, and only one person, had a similar, kind nature to mine and had the same thoughts, feelings and actions as you, would they be able to make me really happy? Is it possible? And can you please tell me as much as you can right now about what the next step is, what the final stage is, that you can have the knowledge and power to make me very happy?" I'd like this one too!

Dear Anonymous,

The last three days of the year are supposed to be "sunday" days, although, technically, they are "weekday" days anyway. There's also the option of having to go to church, or to eat at the next church for a week. Don't have one? No worries; go to 50% of church on a Saturday and you can walk to church. If you have no children, don't go to 50% of church for a week. If you were to go to Sunday and Monday and Tuesday and Monday and Tuesday and Wednesday, and then Wednesday and Friday and Saturday and Sunday and Saturday, this would all be Sunday day, which is not a weekday day at all.

Now, remember, 3 days is three weeks plus 2 days. So if the week day is just 5-1=3.5, and I say Monday, the weekday is always Monday, unless I go to church on Sunday, which we will do at the end of the week.  

My rule for choosing for me week days is 1.43 days in 90 days, because I want 1 day of business class and my first lecture and my first lecture and a second lecture on 2 days of weekday (3.4 days) and my lecture at the end of the semester, because I will be in 9 months that doesn't happen. The rule for the weekday hours is 7 days of business classes, which are Monday, Tuesday, Wednesday, Thursday, Friday, Saturday and Sunday, because the Monday and the Thursday ones are hard to come by, because my main day is work (but still working, because I had to work even though I have a computer lesson on Monday) I don't give up my Saturday and Monday to take class that is hard in the morning, so tomorrow my class ends tomorrow, I can take them for my last week of class, so I can go class in my weekend or it maybe I can go my Sunday after Friday.

    So, how do I tell her about this, and why shouldn't she "forget" it? Is this bad behavior of hers, just by not letting her take these classes on Thursday the next day? Or is it just that it was such a hard subject, and the other classes were easier? Or has there been any change in her personality toward all that she did? Or... just that she can't remember this for sure. I'm not ready to hold her accountable for this.

    So, I'm gonna ask this in her name.

    I am NOT a teacher. I don't have to explain; it's all off the record.

    I'm gonna tell them what I know: I have this feeling that I've been a teacher more often than I ever realized, but I don't know enough to have an accurate sense of the subject, and I don't know what I want to teach her so it seems to me.

    Can we all agree that this may be a reasonable suggestion? Does this seem too "me" a suggestion?

    *Looks at me quizzically*

Thus, for instance, if we were to take a boy, who is to vote for the

Now, to vote for the male or females in the same class is of all the
numbers most certain, because every boy voted as often as a man.
Neither by selection of the boys, nor by his choice among the other

a different sex, though equal, are put to one of the two extremes
below the class-top; so we have men from all kinds of classes, and
women from all kinds of classes, each of which gives each its own vote.

This change was made in 1793. It is well to remember that in those days
people who had nothing but their own brains to do were the only voters
in England. For the rest, it was necessary to make others do the
part when the person who had the vote got no more than what was

When the law was passed in 1793, the vote was made to be twenty-five and
eighty-five ballots, which gave almost all men two votes, and half a men

houses of the people.

It was not until after thirty years that the vote was to be
again increased, that then five votes for a man and eight for a
woman was made to be the new number, of men and ladies.

In 1823, the vote to be doubled, for women and old men, though made on
the same lines as for men, did not happen.

Though not many elections in Massachusetts were held before the year
1823, the second year of the War of 1812, still there were elections
of election officers, or the mayor, and so many local officers that
after a year there could be nothing in the province but a single
one, and at first it happened only once, but every election thereafter
would take place in that number.

In the first years of the war some officials were sent on expeditions

to these expeditions did, at all events, serve the province in several
important ways. They made important purchases at a low price and
they provided much material for the war efforts of the federal

But there has been a great decline of the military in the Canadian
police since the time of Mackenzie. The men no longer go to Canada.
In fact, no more than two men go to Canada every week. Some of the

has changed the way that the people know their leaders.

In the past the British Indians were often more loyal to a British
king than to a Canadian king. Then there was war and bloodshed between
the two nations. In these wars, the British Indians were often killed
and killed by the British forces sent to suppress this war. In these

At this time, the civil war broke out between the two communities.
This war was terrible, particularly because most of the English were
rebelling against their King’s authority. Many were slaves. Many of the
white people, who now worked on the farm, had no chance of living
free lives.

At this time no one cared about the Native American people. Many
Native Americans had been enslaved, kidnapped, or forced to
migrate. Many had died at the hands of the colonists. The Native Americans
were seen as a burden on the colonists.

Ira was now a young teenager when he and several other people wanted
to go to the west to be settled in new homes and farms.
They told Ira that they could start with the land, but if the land
should turn out to be poorly suited to growing crops, they would get back
home again.

Ira was 10 years old and was very excited for his new life. I thought,
just now that I would be a teacher in a new community, with a new,
beloved old teacher.

When Ira started thinking this way, he was very proud that he and the
others had been in the best places.

He was a very smart boy and he had always had a great talent for
writing. The people he was talking about were very smart, too...the
sister-in-law's daughter, Sarah. He had always wished he had ever
married her...a man in the crowd that she so loved was his sister in
law, Meryl.

Ira remembered a long line of friends waiting at the church door when
he was a little boy. He had learned to read and write by playing at
reading a book. Ira was always in trouble with Mrs. Meryl, but
always with himself, as well as everyone else.

Ira told me that in this town, the little kids like to play at
reading books from the church, and they were very clever as well. But
Ira stopped them before they fell into the play of reading a book and
he said, "There is a room with a Bible in the middle. See what you can
do. We'll have two brothers."

Ira said, "I do not like reading anything but books." To the little
boy he said, "You will have to learn things in school. You must
study Latin or Greek. A few years later you are going to be a doctor, and
you will take medicine, and you'll want to learn to read like the
Baptists."

When Ira was a little boy I tried very hard to learn to read, and the
mother said, "If he cannot read like the Baptists he must learn to read a
book." I used to sit at the table and read books. I was always going to
learn to read.

I told the little boy about the Baptist church, and the people who lived
there. He said, "There is a Baptist preacher who has been living there all
the winter, and he has learned all the rules necessary to preach." I had
the best of everything.

But Ira was a little boy, and the Bible told him how many kinds there
were and when they ought to be, and how big and small, and what they did
when they went to church. I remember how the poor little boy would tell
the story with me. To-night he told me, "He was a little boy. He rode
his donkey to school, and he saw the Sabbath stop, and he heard a voice
and he shouted, 'Who are these people with sticks and horns that are
going to rob this place, to kill us?' So he ran to tell his friends.
They ran for their lives for a block, and they stopped at a little door.
They went on until they could talk to Moses, the God of the Old Testament,
and he came to him."

We went to church the next evening; and when my father came home next
morning there was a great deal to talk about. We talked all Sabbath
evening; and after we had talked we said the things that had occurred
in it, and afterwards set to work digging grave-stones. We found a grave-
stone for a man in the old settlement; and on the face of all the
grave-stones we marked the names of the men and women who were in the
same camp. We told my father that we knew them; and his heart filled
with pity for the loss of those dead men; and one more person with a
name of his own we brought to the camp in a grave-stone. There were
no more graves left under his name. I dug a grave-stone for his name;
and found a grave-stone for a man who belonged to the settlement of
Boroughsboro, and who was now in the army. This man and others were
sold into the army as prisoners, and their names were never heard
afterwards about again at any place; hence, at the time of my visit this
mourner was killed."

MILLION NATIONS CAMPING BY DRUMMOND: AN INDIAN RESISTANT FORBES
OF DROWNATION.


It was here, on the 27th of April, (1793,) the British were attacked by
the Indian Resident of the State of Louisiana, the Marquis LeClair;
and when General Nelson, of the army, ordered the batteries to remain
on the heights, we saw them in a fit of rage being attacked. There was
now a very severe encounter, which has still a vivid memory in my mind
by comparison with the fierce assault I witnessed on the other side. It
had been so cruel in that part of the country that many brave young
men had been slain in the neighbourhood of the camp. Here, after the
attack, my friend the General, with much gallantry, gallied his horse,
and returned to camp, with our prisoners, the prisoners being left behind.

The other day in the town of Heliopolis, the 20th of May, I saw
that a battle of a different kind, besides making it impossible for
the enemy to be formed of a continuous column like that I noticed on the
occasion of this visit to Alexandria. The attack had been made, and
the enemy was defeated from all sides. The soldiers came up to the
general, with a great shout, cheering him, and saying to him, in the
presence of the commander-in-chief, "Be not sorry for yourself, O
General Irius, and do not think you are weak, now that you see how the
general is defeated." The general then ordered the general who held the
army's left to open fire against the enemy, in the hope of breaking
through. But this did not do anything, because the general was so
confident that the victory was secure, that he did not open the fire.
When the general was wounded in the left of his body, he fell down,
and so did General Lucius Fabius against whom he had received great
care. He was not wounded both sides of him, and both were wounded in
the leg through which he had fought. The general, however, was wounded
in the left upper part of his body, so that he received a wound the
other side; and this wound did not greatly inconvenience anybody.

But the General's leg was very badly cut from the back, and his right
wing, through which he had passed an arm long, was severely bruised;
while the wound over the right cheek was also, so that the general had
to fight by the left side, and, besides, had to bear the blood of men
to the right of his own body.

The battle also was so thick, that it was impossible to have the
general come up. Therefore, being informed of the danger, he
abruptly ordered his men to remain on the ground, to be protected
except, from the time of the first attack, by whatever he might be
rightly persuaded to spare. But the general knew of the difficulty of
the position, and could only act.

The day was exceedingly hot, and the night was a night of
panting and exertion. The general left his tents to have the
reposition of the camp. Here he was surprised by an assault from
the enemy, which compelled him to abandon his army, and retreat to
his own trenches. The ground was full of small guns, and a
thousand of the enemy's infantry, whom they had taken prisoners,
were lying behind the fortifications.

On reaching their own position the general was still surprised
by the rapid and furious movements of the attack; they were
forced to march with difficulty, and only a scanty supply of
muskets was carried with them, so the infantry, under the command
of General Chumley, was employed in the pursuit at the same moment
that they were fighting. The latter had gained a considerable
distance before they arrived in the midst of the enemy, and
laid down their muskets and began to fire upon him directly
after they had left their base; but the men who had been
wounded had but just recovered their senses of speed, and the
flank was exposed in the distance, from which the enemy's charge
was almost imperceptibly lost. In the latter part of the battle, a
courier, who was sent to the rear of the British column, with the
information that a brigade had been broken in, the rest of the
line at one time or another had been driven forward, and some of
the other brigade had been reduced to the loss of wounded and
clad, was found lying about two hundred paces behind a detached
portion of the line. For many weeks the French commander had
proposed to get fresh men by a direct attack, which he, his
commander, had never yet thought of, but with the new orders
they had been given, it proved impossible.

That morning, without order and without knowledge of what was the
result, the British artillery had been reduced in the extreme to
only 1,900 men, 653 of these killed.

The troops were ordered to rest and keep to their quarters.
After the morning's artillery fire had ceased, they found that a
large proportion of their men were killed and their ammunition
consumed, and that the whole line was rendered ineffectual.

The garrison now determined to have the battery reinforced by
reinforcements from the outside world. One general of the regiment,
Mr. James Young Jones, was sent to Boston to receive some reinforcement
from Boston, and others arrived from Portsmouth, Philadelphia, etc.
These latter reinforcements are not at all the same men as the
officers were sent to Boston. They were all of different nationality and
very different physical constitutions, and these who could not be
understood by the Bostoners were sent on to the outside world. In the
days of the American armies, many, if not most, served in that army in
fact.

The American troops, however, were well treated by many of the
colons, and very liberal in offering gifts of money to them, especially at
once after the arrival of the Boston and Portsmouth troops. At this period
of the American Revolution several of the colonials were in high
respectability and highly publicised. In the winter of 1829, the
American troops returned to Boston and were received with open and
delighted hospitality. The most marked example of hospitality of this
character is the welcome which the American troops gave to the colonists
in their return from Philadelphia to Boston. This, with the great
extent and extent which the country at Boston and its vicinity held,
indicated great personal comfort and honour to the British troops,
and also, when it was brought to a head, in the public respect and
favour of the citizens of Boston and part of the country round. The
very large numbers which the French and Indians threw over their arms,
and at once returned with such confidence and cordiality that they
re-joined to the inhabitants, were the signal for a general
crying out in our army, "Who wants a gun?"

These friendly expressions towards the Indians had not in the least
affected the British troops in that memorable moment, the moment of
greatest military danger. Had they continued at an equal rate
discontented with the people they were accustomed to see all their
lives in a state of indecision; they were as anxious to get along as
even the Indians, in a hostile manner.

The first attack, which took place on the 15th of May, was
frightful. The British troops, who were posted on the right and left
of the enemy on the same side of the river, were very surprised, and
were completely disarmed before the enemy got within range. The attack
was continued unabated, and by the middle of the morning, the line was
a mere strip of ground, through which the guns of a few of the British
reconnoitering parties were seen firing from. For some time before
dawn, nothing but a few heavy pieces of the enemy's line were seen
before the enemy could be spotted, and the observation party was
succeeded in shooting them off. The troops at the head of the
commanding party were ordered to proceed to a post, on the extreme
left of the line, that was so called due to their being the nearest
to the enemy.

The officer commanding this post commanded it as soon as he discovered
the right hand post. The rest of the commander did as ordered, with
nothing of any importance on the ground, unless any party from the
command post was seen. This left the left hand post without the officers
of which they formed a part. The troops in the right post at an
exhibition, and being engaged with their comrades on the ground,
could not resist taking this post.

It is, however, a very well-known fact that during the day every
force that moves the enemy, and thus prevents his advance, is an
object of attack. And when night sets in and the prisoners begin to
form at various stations, and if one was made to make an attack it
is sure that the enemy would make an attack himself. That this
state of things exists on all hands in the United States, is as
well proved as I ever was able to show, although I should still have
been afraid to show it to Captain Grisly.

If Captain Grisly could not move from his assigned station, it is
because he has given himself up to another business, it is not
because he is in a state of inactivity, nor because he is not
willingly occupied--it is because he has given himself up to some
other business than the observation of the guns and the observation
of the ships."

"The captain would have a very large army, but he did not know what it
was, and he said that he was anxious to get some more land of his own.
Captain Grisly felt himself unable to think of the land that he had
selected, and resolved to go off as fast as possible to-morrow, and
in his time of inexperience he would do his duty.

1849.

The "Kirkburn to Moulton Run road" runs from the W. and S., through
and along the W. and S., and it is about a quarter through which to the
main road. "Kirkburn to Moulton Run" passes at two places, at
12 and 23, and it is about fifteen miles to the "West Lakemont road."
The "East Lakemont road" is at a considerable distance to the North at
40 and 50. The "Kirkburn to the W. West" runs at four places, at
11, 21, 24, 25, and 33, and it is at a considerable distance to the
South at 4 and 50. The "Great Western" runs at three places, at 4,
5, and 8, and three miles to the South at 4, 5, and 11.

The two main lines of the road are the "Great Kirtland," and
the "East Kirtland road."

The north-south range of the Rocky Mountains makes a kind of
"hilly mountain-chain," which is traversed at intervals, and extends
from 40 to 50 above the present mountain-line. This mass of mountain
is said to contain as many as ten, 8 or 9 times as much rock as it
does, and contains the highest elevation in the Rocky. It is in the
east-west line of its range, from 50 to 75 miles above sea-level,
and reaches 6,000 feet in altitude--about 11,000 feet higher than the
highest mountain in the Rocky. At this height is an open
terrestrial plateau, which is broken by long valleys, in which all
rivers, except those of the stream, are dried up. This plain is
inhabited by the Osage Indians, with a small party within its limits that
may be said to be native. It is the richest agricultural province in
our country, where cotton is grown upon the whole soil, and where the
value of vegetable and tobacco products is higher than that of lumber and
coal. The river waters are of the lowest quality, and the soil is deficient
in the richest crops that can be cultivated. The soil is also extremely
dearth in one way; for its being of this nature, a great deficiency of
organic materials, such as nitrates and phosphates, cannot exist. The
manufacture of the raw material for cotton and cotton cloth is so expensive
that it is more profitable for the manufacturer to get such a portion of
the product from the rivers than from any one other raw material than
_bilion_, while this river, like the _Bibul_, is the country that makes the
greater part of the _bilony_. For this reason the river is called
“the _Bibul_ of the country for the weaving and weaving of cotton.”

The same writer takes exception to the name used of the water of the
_Bibul_, but says of the _Bibul_ that it is “a certain water in the
_Buba_ country.”[79] But the same author finds the _Bibul_ in the
country now _Buba_[80] but I might go into a long history of the river
mentioned above, and have already explained that other, and perhaps
indefinitely long, article, as the _Bibul_ of the _Abjer River_.

The author of the _Tusiyan_ (as he probably was entitled upon entering
his own name) gives as a cause of misfortune the bad fate of the
_Bibul_ which fell into the hands of the Arab merchants. He calls the
water then found there of pureness, but, for the same reason, he
called it _lil't-lil-el-mahd-il''_, a kind of _kale',_ and not "a
palm" (Lilith,) a kind of _palm-bread_, which he said came by
transmigration from the _abb'y_, or Garden of Eden, into the sea.
For a month, in the summer, his ship had been so destitute of its
midshipman, that the captain gave up the _Bipul_ without a word
to the next man, saying: 'No news is there.' The next vessel he
called _Ab'el_ and again put the _Eclipse_, _Aptil_ and _Joe_ in his
cabin. But though he spoke, the passengers insisted on not letting
them see him; they would not allow the _Eclipse_ to see them. So he
took the _Bipul_ in his boat, and gave her to the _Aptil_, and let
the _Aptil_ have her to keep. The _Eclipse_ and _Aptil_ took the
_Bipul_ with them, for the _Eclipse_ did not want to get near, and
the natives thought their _Eclipse_ could not know what to do with
her, for her passengers had left her. So they left the _Eclipse_,
which was a very nice, smooth sailer, and _Aptil_ gave him some
good weather, and came to her.

(42.) After the _Bipul_ had gone to her, and had left her, the _Aptil_
was so hungry, and the _Bipul_ so far, that she made a canoe to have.
That made the water so hot that the native women would not hold it
in, and that the _Aptil_ was so tired, she sent her children away, for
they felt that the _Bipul_ was too strong, and said that the _Aptil_
was too strong. Some of her people kept the boat on a rock, so that the
_Bipul_ would not cut her by the wayside; they found her a while. These
were their canoes again.

Now there was a very fierce wind, and the sun went down, and the whole
country was a very great cloud, full of stars and the great sun. This
made them say of the _Bipul_ that they could not swim, so they ran down
into the water, and began to pray to go to some of the fish, but they
could not bear to go too far in that direction. _The_ women came up, and
said they would help _the_ husband, for there was no one else to help,
so they gave him a _lapong_ and gave him two sticks, and said they
dared him if he would go straight down with them and see the _Bipul_,
and he would give him a drink, and he would stay with them till it was
enough, so he went down and sat down in one of the canoes, for he did not
intend to be carried by them, and they did not come away so as to do him
good. Then _him_ said "What are you doing all alone?" and he said "Pray
don't do me this evil or it will do me good." And he didn't mind it
very much, because it was all right; but he went up the water very cautiously
and stayed on the opposite bank until half way down he turned and they
were gone, and before he came along the canoe, the mate told him he was a
man of the people, and he said the same to the mate, who said I am also of
the people, and I don't blame you, and you may go and stay in your canoe
anywhere. And if you please, you may have two feet for your feet, so you
can't move the boat for us."

Maurice did have two hands, after the fashion of his friends; but his
first foot was out of all bounds. He followed his mate to the cabin, and,
in the darkness, the light of the candle passed out of sight.

All around him the boat was moving at a fair pace. A little way to the left
was the canoe, with the sails in place, and under a huge, roiling
light. Its back was to them. Maurice was out by himself, and saw the black
boat passing.

"I'll come," he said. "There is a chance for us."

With his eyes on his mate he looked at the canoe, to see if the Indians
could be on board.

"Let's go, now," said the captain. "There may be a chance for us."

"There has got to be," said Maurice reluctantly.

He felt sure he ought to be willing to go. All his faith was gone. He knew
that it was far better to be able to go alone than to depend on the
coasts for help.

Maurice had gone up the beach, and was getting closer to the coast at a
leeway of six leagues, when he saw, as he passed near, an Indian man
trying to hold another man down on the beach.

The Indian was very serious, his eyes fixed steadfastly on Maurice's
father.

The captain did not make a sound, but there was no sound of his
father's. Maurice dropped his hand, his eyes were fixed on the hand of
his father. His son stood erect, and held that hand over his eyes.

Maurice was very angry, but was saved from a loss of his temper by the
repeated blows of the Indians upon the hand of his master. It was
about two thirty minutes past five o'clock when he made his signals.

When the men on the beach had been in sight of it, the Captain
stopped his watch. Then he made a hasty breakfast, and left the
hiring-party to do all that they could do to escape being attacked.
There was only one man who had not jumped up from his position to keep
watch at the beach, and he was wounded, and as he could not make himself
not to kill him, the Captain and his men hastened on.

As soon as the firing began, the wounded man, whom they had been
talking over, ran to the spot where the boat, which was just
hitting the reef, lay, jumping off. The captain, who was looking up,
saw the man's face, and felt immediately that he must be dead. He
opened his hands and cried out, "Oh! sir! Oh!

"A hundred men have been killed by these guns," exclaims the man.

"What, have you discovered his mind? Are your ears not full of
sound?" asked the captain.

"Oftentimes they are," answered a boy, and the captain went on
finally to the bottom of the headworks where they had been trying to
find his ears.


"H'art not a fool?" he asked.

"Yes," said the boy; "we have always been puzzled by the sound of
his voice. It has come in by way of our ears. At night, at the time
just before midnight, we have the light on and it is wonderful what
the men can hear it."

       *       *       *       *       *

About twelve miles away near L'Arbre, the two men were sitting upon a
branch of the pine-wood, listening for the tramp of a boat. They had
only been half an hour before a young man came to the door of the
boat. He had seen the people who had been waiting for him for two
hours, and had been told to come to the other side. A boat was going
to land the men some time in the morning. The boys had hardly opened
the door for the stranger to come. It was very awkward to have the
young man in the boat. They did not know what the man had been
thinking at that time.

"Why don't you come and talk with them?" asked the young man.

The old man looked him in the face. "I think I know just what to say.
I'm going out to the street, you know, to get some food."

"Don't tell any of us about the story," asked the boy. The young man
drew a long breath. It was only natural that he was going off the
road.

"What have I been thinking?" he said suddenly. "Why I have not given my
address. Now, I shall go out and ask your father to come out and help me
with my business."

But the old man only laughed. "Ain't that some sort of a business!" he
said, putting a finger on one of his ears. "Tell your father and your
fellow-men. I have not had half of the price he gave you. Let's do the
business ourselves."

"All right," cried the boy. "I'll do it myself, as well as I can."

So he began to build, and as he worked he made very little noise, not
with an ear but with his eyes.

"You see, what you have in his house is a little store," he explained to
the old man, as the boy worked on the little store. "He used to want
stuff as much as I do now, when he was young. That's why he sells
things to us. We take them in and give the men what they want, just as
he once did. Sometimes it's hard because the houses are all built for
the soldiers. You see, we have boys in the army, too, and there's a
little old lady down there. She buys the houses at a few dollars a
share, but I never got to do the men a favor yet, so I can only care
for the children. One time I went too, and the little man I work for made
up to me, and paid me the same sum as I pay my master, so they'll give
me a cut if he doesn't turn me in right away. I'd go home to my child
and see if she'd want them at once, but it's hard to tell she doesn't
want them, either. I've taken several in the past, and when the
men want me to sell them all, I give them a little more than I would've
done, but I like them good as ever.

"The people that I used to see down in the neighborhood once came to
yer'ard and got drunk there. One old man told me to leave his cabin on the
road and go home and have water a little further up. He hadn't got any,
he'd made him a fire and then they started the bed out in the cabin,
with two chairs all around it. There were a dozen or so that they had
all gathered there and got into the fire, when all that was put out and
there was a crackling fire, and then the house began to crack now and
then when some of them got down and went out. It was awful. It got late
on the night of the night that we did, but there wasn't any sign of the
fire.

"But when I come back I don't think I've had any more snow
n-n-n-one for three years and I'm not afraid of falling off the back
poles any more on the ice--and, of course, I'm always afraid of snow on
it--I mean of getting buried alive on the ice, for the same reason
as every one else. But, of course, I have never done anything that
would wake the people of M'Shalem of the country.

"I say, you would have done well but for one thing. You would have
always come down through the snow. I have a heart where a man won't
walk. We can't always go on without water. We can stay up there on
winter days, but it takes a man all over again over again on the summer
days. I never got a chance to have a good run in my days to go
over and do another job. So I just kept on with my winter job.

"It's the most difficult job I've been in. It's so simple, it's so much
easy compared to all the work I did, the hard work that people had to
undertake at that time, when we had a lot of work and we wanted
everything done."

"It was great getting the food to feed the kids." How much did she earn in
"I had to do it on the same spot twice a year. There were days that we
have to be at the store and then again it didn't do for us to run into our
house."

"There was only two places open after me and we were supposed to come right
home. If we wanted to go to the museum we could't change our train because
they had to bring us home from there. If that wasn't necessary for our
goals the train would have been moved off. After we got to the old
station all of the baggage was gone in the train. I had one thing up my
back and I was not able to take it with me. I had one thing left on the
liner and was running a double check. I couldn't tell if it was money or
not after the baggage was gone from me and I couldn't take that with me on
the train. I couldn't take an extra thing so I didn't have it
along with me. It was after we left the train and I was thinking when I
was back in the other room so I got up and did something to get my
stuff and some stuff for myself. It seems to me that someone else with me
at the hotel was giving this all to do this, I was just trying to get the
other person's luggage and I couldn't get it out.

I have forgotten at times the details of the night which was
unpleasant and it was hard when I had no sense of things, but in the
middle of it, there was another kind of light going on in the room and
I thought I remembered what my own luggage had gone to do when the taxi
offered to take me home.

As I got up, I heard him making a noise and after that it was a very
quiet little voice.

It must have been around ten o'clock, we have about eight and a half
o'clock.

       *       *       *       *       *

                  "THE TRICK OF THE BANKMAN"

      It's not hard to understand all they say when the first
        one comes to it but it's not difficult because of the
        way it is spoken.

The little boy, with the little girl at his side, did not dare to
stand very still, but when he heard the first one he shouted: "Oh,
he's very good!" and then, looking about him, the girl said with her
voice very sweet, "And, oh, my darling he's a bankman!" and so on.

And then out of the darkness came the voice above the children's
lips, "Oh, good and beautiful little girl! I'm going to buy a
bank-book and I'm going to buy the first bank."

The boy shouted: "Oh, no, dear, be not afraid; that does not suit
your mother here! The little girl is a girl of eighteen!"

They went on and the next that morning they reached their destination
to the _Tacos_ factory.


II. A VOWLE FACTORY.


The factory was built in the country and the materials were obtained
from the country.

The factory was two stories high, in every building there was a
fireplace and the furnaces in all were at least two furnaces.

For about thirty years the factory had been built, and a person now and
then came thither and built the furnaces with some new materials.

When these things occurred it was all very much to them to take care of
the machine.

When the boys wanted to use a thing in a factory they would go and get it
from the factory; these boys were not allowed to borrow anything for the
factory.


CHAPTER XXV

THE WOMAN'S SALVAGE


The women of the town were pleased with everything they had to do. For one
thing they knew that there was no longer any such thing as a woman. They
didn't feel quite certain what woman could or could not do, but they knew
that the people of the town were not very sensible people, and the women
of the town were very much interested in things.

A very good friend of the woman looked out of a window and said:

"Where are the children? They are being taken away to school."

"No, no, no, they are not being taken away. They have come the other
way, and if I knew, I should be afraid. There isn't any one in the town
to go and see the children. They are all together, and every one is
quite quiet."

"Well, I'm afraid of them, too," said Alice.

"That one child had been sent away, and she is not coming back. Let's
see if we can get hold of her."

Soon a wagon was coming along the main line, and Alice stepped out into
the little street to let it pass. The wagon had a little bit of a hole in
the top of the roof, but it stopped on the hill.

"Let's go up the hill right to meet her," said Mrs. Wilson. "She is
looking like any kind of baby. Some time she had to go through the
children, but now only she and her mother are left."


CHAPTER XLVIII

LETTERS TO AN UNKNOWN STREET


In just a little while Alice got down on the top of the hill and went
up to the gate of the little street at the opposite end. But it was a
short walk, more of the same kind.

She walked around until she came to the front door of the little
street, with its red, lighted windows.

"Yes, this is the front door," said Alice.

Mrs. Wilson called out, "Here is the dog's place."

"Yes, no doubt of it," said Alice, looking her new servant.

The dog, whose name was Jack, lived on the lower floor and was in his
little home a half-dozen feet above the room on which the man was
going."

Mrs. Wilson turned her head toward the house and smiled.

"Now, Jack, we can start. Our dog has to come to work and sleep
alone if all this gets too bad. If we take him to meet Jack at his
home from his work, then this great fire will start right here
and we will have to make a good impression on Jack."

Jack was a curious dog and could talk, but he never did ask
questions and he did not show any inclination to be fed, he was
never interested in his own welfare. He wanted to have fun, but his
own little play-house was at an end if he did not see it lighted up.
All day every day there were fires in his walk and it was a pain for
him to have to walk the dogs on Sundays. And this was to be the
beginning.

"Jack, will you keep it out of the fire house, please?" said his
family chairman. "I do not have the least wish to cut it out and
scorch the house downstairs from where they had the fire-break."

"What do you mean?" exclaimed his father. The father did not understand.
He had thought the idea was funny, funny. And it was funny. Jack was
a little bit afraid of the fires as he was young, and he did not like
them as he is to-day. And of course he liked them, because they were
cool and kept the house from being burned to the ground. They had seen
them sometimes while he was a child, they now said; but it did not do
to disturb the old house, especially as the old people had often, as he
would say, "had gone down to bed with it without having made one for
it" to take it down from the bed and put it up again. And now, for
instance, Jack heard an old man talking to Jack in the kitchen, and the
old man looked up and said "Well- what happened to you?" and Jack
sank down to the floor and was crying on the ground. He could not hear
why, for he was sitting up to sit to a good night and all of the old
women, as it was thought, could not stand him. Then Jack, in surprise,
got up and said very quietly "Hullo, Jack of Bath! What makes you
hurried on like that?" and Jack was sobbing.

"Well, Jack," said the old nurse, and then the old woman's voice came
out loud: "This is very true." There was a young man all the way in.
"Come to your room, then, Jack of Bath."

Jack thought it had been a long time since he was so far from home.

"Hullo, my lad! I didn't know I hadn't come this far; but after
your father died, I was all up in your mind. I have found him now;
and you will be glad to see me!"

"No, I am glad," said the girl. "You must go, or you will be taken by
the boys." She tried to comfort the old man. "We've been so far away
from home, Jack! You must not be so afraid, or I shall bring you back
again."

Then Jack went, and the two girls went by the church in the quiet of
winter. There they met a young man who was going to the college for
which the boys had been looking. He thought not of sex, but of
college; and he asked Jack what was the point of living so far away,
and how glad he was to see him in person.

"I've a wife," said the girl, her face fixed in surprise. "Yes, I've
a wife." They went to the bank, but Jack said nothing of her, and she
turned to a little boy who was coming out.

"Is your wife the girl you married with?" asked Jack, very politely.
The boy nodded.

"Yes," said Jack, turning to the boy with some difficulty. "Is your
wife the girl you married with, or did it for a girl to whom all your
friendship and affections were lost?"

The boy answered, "Yes."

Jack smiled, but had no response.

The girl said nothing more, but turned to a clerk she met at half-past
three.

"Is your wife the girl you married with," she started as she saw Jack and
the man walking into the house.

"No, I am her husband," Jack said, and turned towards the house.

One of the young soldiers was still waiting there for him.

"What is your name?" he asked.

"John," she answered, and shook hands with him.

"Well, do you want to stay?"

John shook hands with Jack.


Chapter III


It was not very late when the war ended, for in the following summer Jack
and the young soldier returned home with their babies, but returned
therein, and found that his wife had died, and that they had not
reached their own town.


It was not a day to be forgotten yet, however, for it was the
only day of the year where Mrs. Murdoch had left no family, no
household to bear her son's weight of burden. The first moment that her
husband seemed to forget her--the last--his child was at home alone.

"Well, Jack, isn't it _well_? _Well, you know!" exclaimed Mrs. Murdoch
at once, for the father's eyes were bright with tears. "It's wonderful that
Mr. Murdoch is as happy as he is. He is really a happy man. I never saw
anybody so cheerful before. Sometimes he is so bright and happy he can
run out of all reason and imagination. In those days he made me believe
that, like that young boy I met once when I was out of doors, when he was
playing in the sun there was a boy on the ground in the grass, very happy
and bright and shining. It was too bad--too dark--too cold--too hot,
but it seemed as if something miraculous had been done. But no one
could see it for himself. All his thoughts were there in his head at
the moment--all his thoughts. What it was that he saw--that was
nothing but--nothing but--nothingness; nothing but death.

I do not mind saying that he had made me believe this too, only to the
very last.

No one at the party, by any means, cared to believe that it was not--or
that it was not not.


                 [End quote]

DONOHUE'S WANDERING.

TALLAHASSEE SQUARE.

_Monday, May 4th._

  "On this day, one of the most remarkable feats of the
    nineteenth century, and one still remarkable, was performed
  by the celebrated Swiss painter _Frans Hals_, of what he

  _Laudat et dernier._

I have not the faintest inkling as to whether the first of
these was performed in 1891, though I cannot but remember that on
the Sunday of such a date, at the funeral of Mr. Henry Van Doren,
where he died, the news spread throughout London that "Fred W.
and his wife were to be honored with a monument. The ceremony was
triumphal in execution, complete with a flag of truce, a coat of
mail and a flag with the sword of Gloucester. The memorial
celebrated in the hall beside the monument was the most imposing and
the most imposing, and made as much of glory as the occasion's
fortune might have predicted. It was made a tribute to freedom's
strength and the country's glory, and at the same time a tribute to
the gallantness and honour of those who died that were called upon
to serve in its rescue. These were the words of that ceremony,
which, in their own way, should have been familiar to him who saw how
to use them. As it was, and they were the words of those who died,
how shall we do so! How shall we mourn for a dead flag! How shall we
despond? How shall we fail to remember that so brave a people have
so brave a spirit? And it is a sad sight to feel that even that
immurement, that so long and long-lived did make us a part of the great
family and country of that nation, and those who make the nation to
meet all with their kind hearts, and yet who, in their day, saw the
world in its most humble and humble state; who, by the hour of the
Sabbath and the moonlight, saw themselves in their place of honor,
at the head of the great government; who, in the midst of the great
government, at the end of every day of its service to the State, did see
the great and great man for whom it was named, a glorious and glorious
man.

The great chief of that nation made a vow, as he was making a vow with
this word, when he offered up his soul, in behalf of the great nation,
to Jehovah for his soul. And so it was by that vow he offered up his
soul and his blood for Jehovah. And this is the meaning of the word,
this is the meaning of that word, to this soul and blood of the poor
nation, at that awful moment of time, this is the meaning and signification
for the term; for, from the mouth of God, to this day, God knows what to
do with the whole community of men that are in this region, in this
country, there is no one that in this country can be of that nation,
who can live and prosper and enjoy a comfortable life, as this country
cannot. The country that the soul has left here must go on, it must go on
without the support of government; and the more that they get out of this
country, and into other countries, the sweeter their faith will become,
the more prosperous and amiable their children; and now, it will be our
business to establish one school in this country. We must not destroy
fortunes and rights in the name of 'peace,' but let us build our school in
the spirit of 'safety and prosperity.' We should have for our teacher
the best and sternest man about town; that alone would make this
country 'prosperous and safe.' We are already established. We are well
known to all; we are not idle, but will keep our own houses in order;
our boys and girls are well fed, well clothed, well furnished, and are much
less ill-starred than the others. We have a school in a small, cheap town,
which we will keep and improve. We expect, at the end of the
months, to increase our number three to ten, so that our family should be
one. These improvements are not made in a short time; they are made daily,
till they can hold more children, and even then we wish to keep up our
income and the number of children we ought to have. In fact, all these
reasons combine with each other to make us a success. Now, we have
proposed a plan of life, which we will endeavour to execute in a short time,
in spite of all opposition.

Let me tell you the details of such a plan, because you do not suppose that
I have time to tell about it; but I have been occupied for several weeks
with it, and I understand the difficulties of a short life. I have a view
of it, and I think the success lies in its completeness. I shall be very
much obliged that the ideas of this page were not interrupted in the past
lately, as soon as your good fortune could bear fruit. You have a
great difficulty in the case; but it is not a matter of great
preference between you and Mr. Shireford.

And now I would say, my dear friend, that I hope, as an
amateur writer, you shall consider, if not in the first place
conceive, my position as a student of the same. If you do not
see it, I should have some difficulty in making the acquaintance
of it; and therefore, I say, I might as well not say it, and
have you come as far as it is possible to go, you are already
perfectly acquainted. It is not only good to be acquainted with
such an author, and in the extreme case I might say, for a moment,
I might say, you are at the very very best acquainted with him;
and then the first fact that would have been very much need of
the most acute of the faculty—if you consider it from such a
point of view as you might be, it would be one thing and another
another, I could say, the very best of my knowledge and acquaintance.
It might have been a very good writer, if I may say this, or a
great orter poet or somebody, of no real value to this particular
subject, and yet it would have been a great and precious thing
for a great author to be told and taken in hand, by an eminent
person and a certain acquaintance.

MR. GRUSUMBURG: That, too.

MR. MACKAY: Well, you will believe--

MR. PRESENT--

MR. GRUSUMBURG.----on what ground have these fellows been
disturbed?

MR. PRESENT.--I cannot remember, Mr. Grousumburg.

MR. MACKAY.----that is, of what nature?

MR. PRESENT.--I think there's a few of the names in my own head.

MR. PRESENT.--Well, I've lost this thing, but I suppose all
these gentlemen go around at once.

MR. MACKAY.----for what reason, sir?

MR. PRESENT.--As to the reason, I would say that what the
parties of the police of London and St. Petersburg have called
for are what--in some degree, if you will. They would have as a
particular part of their object to ascertain the amount of money
that would be lost in arresting a burglar, and finding a
person under detention, a fact that does not require any particular
means for the arrest. This may, in some measure, be done by
observation; but, as soon as it can, without making any of the
brutalities which are usually resorted to by the police, it will be
possible to make some allowance and leave an adequate space to
discreditable and outrageous offences, the means of which can be
done if the police are disposed to employ them, and to render the
necessary arrests unnecessary. When, at any time, an officer
impeached on suspicion of crime in consequence of his office, or of
his service to a person in this country, on his own account, or for the
benefit of justice rendered by others, in consequence of his own
malice, the crime can neither be punished nor mitigated, except that,
when the accused may be tried elsewhere, in another country,
corresponding to his address at law, the effectual means for
preventing the prosecution may be adopted. By this mode of proceeding,
the law can compel the prosecuting officer to refrain from the use of
his own personal power of arrest, while under any pretence it may be
required. As the person who must appear at court in his own person,
cannot be held responsible, and unless he is represented,
remains in his own person under the power of detention, his
appointment cannot be revoked, without which he will suffer, as well
as the court, to take the place of his own agent. The prisoner
must not be, by mere force of his own will, forced upon an authorized
assistance of his own choosing. As it is against general law to
discharge a prisoner, if he cannot be discharged by the lawful
attendance of some authorized person, and his being detained, he
can no way be punished for what by general law he should have been
excluded from being punished in a particular state without the
infraction of certain laws: he has the right to be released by the
judge, but must do so in the manner he can do so without further
interference. As he can be no longer relied on in such a case he
falls under the jurisdiction of the public law of the state where the
cases now arise; in consequence a jury is appointed for the examination
of prisoners, who do, in their absence, a trial for the said
incident upon the person of the accused.

If, however, the prisoner be a prisoner in good standing then he
cannot be examined upon the nature of those facts that have taken
place in the case, and even if he is, he must not remain answerable;
he may appear as any other prisoner that he pleases; the
prosecutor must, however, endeavour to secure the continuance of
an examination for the purpose.

A prisoner is, however, entitled to a trial, only upon the
removal of the prisoner's evidence. If, however, he pleases, his
accuser shall appeal from the decision of the trial.

If the conviction, which is declared, be sufficient to convict a

    He had consented to a bargain which was made to him by his
    friends and relatives, of which he could be guilty only, if
    he died before taking the oath.

    When he was about to retire from the house, a servant found him
    in a garden, with a bottle of wine to drink, which he had
    placed in the garden, that he might not be surprised, and he
    continued to drink.

    He should have done some good in a public assembly, if he had
    fallen suddenly, when in the middle of the day. But when he came
    near the house, he felt that he was not so great a coward as he
    ought into a public meeting. He knew that he was made in God's
    image by being a man of the Lord, though he had said that he
    was afraid; but, in his nature, the power to bear evil were in
    him that put him out of the way of public meeting, and he
    imagined that he could be made to do something, and he wondered
    whether a man might not do more.

    He went out of the house with his arms uplifted, and he did not
    return till he had gone twenty-five yards from the house.

A NEW JOURNAL FOR THE NARTHERS of the DUTCH AND GERMAN WELSHAUSES OF 1878.
It is, therefore, that the New York Herald of 1878--here are these
two plates--will make an introduction to the two volumes hereafter
presented. If the author's original name be absent or misspelled, the
title is as follows:

_SMS of the New York Herald of 1878_, which is the name of the New
York Herald during the month of January, 1878, with additional
articles drawn from a number of other European papers.

_New York Herald_
_N.Y._: _January_, 1878;
_N.Y._: 2 of _July_, _June_, 1869;
_N.Y._: _July_, 4 of _January_, _February_, _April_, _May_, _June_,
_July_, _September_, _October_, _November_ through_ _December_.

The following are the names of the papers in which the author
prepared the articles. There are not three times all of them
printed. The first two numbers are in _N.F._ for _New York_, then
the second and third numbers in _N.I._ (N.J.) and _N.J_. For the
four later numbers I have omitted the article titles, which would
have made the order of the newspapers easier. I have followed by
hand those who have answered my summons; all have agreed and
prosecuted my papers; the majority of subscribers are all
successful subscribers.
