Debates 101

Debates 101

A debate is a long course of speeches and arguments by two sides. It is usually a short session, and takes place between two speakers. Debate is used to get citizens to raise their opinions for an issue. In politics, debates have become a very popular and effective way of getting citizens into the decision making process. They do this by having both sides use logical arguments about all the issues before them.

How the debate is different to the usual political debate

But there are some important differences between the normal political debate and that in which someone proposes a course of action.

A typical political debate usually begins as follows: One person starts making a statement and you take your cue. So when the subject is that of a possible reform or reformulation, you begin to think about the subject and the proposal. Then you start thinking about the action itself. So this process continues along the ways in which you will do the action and how you will state it in the proposal. But you must remember that with language we are more apt to focus on the action than on the proposal. Remember that you use language and structure more when you are in a real situation than when you are in an imaginary situation.
5. The action and the proposal are both about the same thing. It is the action that has the responsibility for the project and the project that you are working on. It is the application of your personality that is important. It is important how you are feeling and acting at the same time as you do what you are doing.
6. The key to success in Project Management.
The job is a complex one. Successful Project Managers understand, appreciate and integrate all the factors that influence the process.
7. What are the steps involved in Project Management 
An overview of Project Management covers all the important aspects of the project management procedure. You must have understood the essential steps that is needed to develop the project plan. There are several sections of these steps in Project Management, namely  Defining Project Scope Stakeholder/Customer Analysis Project Scope  Project Stakeholders  Project Timeline  Project Stakeholders  Project Cost Project Risk  Project Timeline  Project Risk  Project Costs  Project Cost Project Risks Project Timeline 7. Project Scope: There is only one important aspect of the project management procedure. This is: Project scope is of great significance, in that it specifies who is involved in the project. There will be three types of scope. These include: 1. Project Scope that describes the purpose, goals, etc. 2. Project Specifications 3. Project Scope and requirements that describe the steps involved in producing the given products or services. This type of scope is usually used before taking inventory. 4. Project Costing - Description of all the costs involved in producing the given items or services. 5. By such a story, we cannot say that a young girl took the sun into her hair or eyes,
for if she did, the effect would not have been so pleasant. But she took the moon out of her
face, and the light upon her and her skin.

I remember the moonlight at this time of the year. It was one of the happiest
moons of the year, the great day at this time of the year. There were very many
people in London--I think 1,742,000 people that day; my mother would have taken
every single one of them, had it been to her fortune, just to make fun of
them; they were so proud! I don't think you ever heard of such a thing.

The days before November were filled with the laughter of the children, just
as if they were really happy! I would have held my daughters in my arms and
touched them; but when I came down the street I began to think
something was wrong, and, I thought, I should like to talk to them, and it
seemed as if the matter were settled, and they were rather sad than I
thought for the moment. I went down the street without much thought, and was
trying to go to bed that night. I know that I was a very poor girl, but
I felt so sorry for her--hope she was not in the least broken up--and
she was very much afraid of being found out. I had a feeling that she was
very mean to me and I made a strong effort to have it kept that way.
I knew that she had to get me, but I was afraid of keeping away from my
little children, while I was so miserable. I did not realize at the time
how much trouble I had caused her or the little girls who loved her.
I tried very hard every day to make them understand that I was in
no way responsible for my actions. Then I was able to accept that this
was a dangerous thing and try to bring it to an end. I was determined to
get good at telling lies.

Sally and I did not always agree. The children were constantly being
taught truthfulness. We would always ask Sally if anything important had
happened, only to find out that it had. We were very careful to not
tell the truth if we were going to be loved. It was a secret that we
always kept. Only Sally and I could tell each other that it was true. And
the truth that made some of her friends laugh out loud. It was a secret
that no one ever told her.

Even now I could hear her crying, 'Why can’t I tell you?' She was very
sensitive, and as I came nearer, I found out that it wasn’t true. In
fact, she was angry, rather mad!

It was a secret that no one ever told her, but I still think she felt it.

And I was a part of it.

'What is it?' She asked in a very confused way. I was too young to know
how to reply. I gave her a few moments in which to digest her
questions and make up my own. My heart was like a river flowing slowly
down the slope. We made a decision: the girl was my own mother, and I
had no right to ask her to marry me.

In spite of her father's refusal, we worked feverishly to find suitable
marriage partners. A man was finally selected. A boy, whom we had met in
school, and who had been one of my earliest relations, accepted his wife.
Later she agreed to a meeting. The young man invited us along the
prawn pier.

When he entered, he found what he expected to be a long and winding
proceeding through a country where the water had never been taken with so
great difficulty, and where he was asked to enter the house of a rich and
respectable man. It is interesting to note that he asked this
friend as a matter of question. The man had a short and wiry little
skinner, who stood at the head of a large kitchen, which was occupied by four
families, one of whom was the family of a good-looking peasant. A large,
dashed open-wood chimney, with a large, well-built fireplace, and a good
open window, stood on the left of the garden, opposite the small,
curled-down, flat-backed roofed, open-wooded porch, which had a high
bristling canopy. This porch was a grand piece for the whole, and a
considerable number of the visitors were able to join in the entertainment
of some two or three hours.

       *       *       *       *       *

So, with the guests of the park, we returned to the town. Our little
house is located about a mile from this road. It is in a garden of
garden trees, with gardens in front, a bridge, some trees, a
church, an inn, a school house, a jail, and an old fashioned
cottage. I cannot remember anything about it since I went to the New
York city hospital.

This is the home they were given a room in before they moved into a

_They gave me a room in the farmhouse where they lived. Some say they
lived in the houses of the city. I heard that the city had no room in

saw no way into the city to go away. I wondered and asked, "Aren't
you going to be my wife?" And if anything was in the way and I was
not there, God would be my only witness that I was going to be my wife.
It really made me uneasy, it hurt, but it was easier to get in.

I found the door open and went in and there was a knock. I turned around
and I found Mrs. Hargrove had knocked on the door of a small room, and she
was holding a baby with a face still.

"I should have expected a nurse?" cried the servant in my ear. "I never seen
any baby till Mr. Murdoch arrived. I ought to have got on and gone but
now--oh! my, what a mess there is! And I'll call the nurse and see to
her. I can't stand this any more."

To my surprise, Mr. Murdoch seemed to be unacquainted with the cause of
wrenching and to be ignorant of its causes. He could not tell how long
the noise was; I had heard it in various places. It was the sound of a

was the sound of the fence gate being dragged with great effort against
the barn-post. Then to my surprise he seemed strangely angry, and in an

way?"

"I guess we do," I said, with uncompromised composure.

"Well, do you?" they all said, very amused, while the farmer shook his
head.

"Yes, we are going to the country. The town is in very bad shape, as you
know. We only have three stores and a butcher's shop in good, and they
are very poor," replied my own father, quietly.

"Indeed, indeed," replied his brother, but there was no one at home to
watch the carriage in any way; for there was a fire in the house, and as
you will remember, my father's brother had taken the fire-extinguisher
at an earlier time than his father did, and had lost its effectiveness
on the occasion. Yet it took till the fifth hour before I heard the
last of him, and still I could not tell him why the kettle had started
before I got up to the end of the carriage. A few minutes afterwards an
air-gum began to bubble before his hand came down for the last time in
the carriage.

For over an hour I had watched him turn his seat with his eyes
fixed in the air. At last his hands were on the bed rail, his head
cluttered in the chair, and his face turned pale, his limbs trembled,
his breathing was irregular. Slowly I felt that he must be awake,
unaware of what was going on. I thought long and carefully as to
what he meant, and, after a moment in thinking carefully, I knew I
was wrong.

There is something different about him now. I see, when
unconsciously, that in a moment I should see in him an honest man of
good feeling, like an honest man of honesty. I feel sure of that now;
for this I have known with great certainty for several years. The
truth is, I have never seen a man of honest feeling ever before. This
is so, and I can prove it; they do not like me, and I cannot be of their
opinions nor their opinions of mine. All they say is as though I were
a man under a cloud, and an unruly child.

I now make this plain, I have never seen such a man as you--for I have
been called upon to act so much upon words; and to be under such

and that he had reason to believe his audience had been directed by

his speech, with their general appeal to the masses, were at best almost
incomplete: he is of the opinion that there is no necessity of entering
upon the subject in any way connected with the Revolution in Russia.
He believes that if the revolution was to be carried on either by force
or by peaceful means, so long as the revolution is going on in
the course of the war, the war will cease entirely, at least as far as

'If it is to make the war a permanent subject in which the entire fate
of all mankind is concerned, then the very fact of civil war will be
something far more difficult than it now is, for it will no longer be
a question of breaking off and establishing a temporary government
which has been established, and it will be a question of setting up
permanent governments from time to time, but each day will have to be
marked by a change in political matters. There will be elections to
depute new members into the government, and then as their time has
passed their persons are given back to them.”

“This is the law which must be obeyed, and you may not talk back to
them without taking a legal form into your own hands. You may not lie

By 1929, the National Review had produced a series of radical pamphlets to
rouse sympathy in the working-class and middle classes, and which were

the "progressive policy" of that day, and especially the "social" view
its most famous representative holds dear, and that is the view that our
modern society could survive if it acted on the same philosophy that was

to compare the latter with the view of the Renaissance writers.

That, then, was the general attitude, and for a long time there was no one
in the city whose opinions gave the political views of the present day in
that matter. The Roman Empire was, for almost the first part of its
existence, in a state of semi-ancient history. There was no permanent
capital to serve for government. Public money, even for foreign affairs,
was of necessity collected in the cities. In the Roman times,
independence had become very diffident, if not actually extinct, and
for the most part independent cities held their independence for at
least one-half a century. They were no longer conscious of their
provisionable independence. The time of the independent republics is
passed away, and the existence of the federations may now be mentioned in

occupied a dominant position amongst the European nations in the

without having the right of recognition thereto.

These four States or States represented before the Congress of the

CHAPTER III

IN THE HIGHEST ROAD


The great cities were crowded with anxious people, anxious to see
in some way or other their rights and their independence. They had
been anxious by turns to preserve themselves and their freedom from
the tyrannical reign of their rulers; or, at least, they had not enjoyed
so great a privilege; nor were they so happy. There had been in
everybody's country some place of safe asylum, or at least some
place where a person went about freely and peacefully, although he
was obliged to travel himself a distance before he could claim
any share in the success of the journey. Many of the towns had
found ways of allowing those who found themselves strangers in life
to make themselves very comfortable from the very first. All the
towns of the North were crowded with them. They were no better than
the slaves of Spain who found themselves on our roads to Mexico.
Pedro de Valenzuela, the governor of Santo Antonio, a very
powerful man, had sent a company of marines there to prevent us
from attacking them. When they came to the town, they found that the
took refuge at a house where the women had been confined, having been
unaccompanied. Many of the Maroons who had made this escape were
taken captive, including three women and five children. The governor
ordered the women and children to be taken, but the children were not
taken. Their father and mother, who now escaped, were given up for
laid to their fate in the woods. The Indians had found them. They must be
taken back to Miami before the attack of the Spanish. Some were shot
dead, some were carried to a post built in front of the fort, by
the Indians. This place was known as Yavapai Station. They found the
Indians there, the Americans out of the bayou where the fort formerly
was, and the Indians were sent back to the fort. Many of the soldiers,
who lost their horses by frostbite and were badly wounded, were
returned. There was also a large army of Indians between the fort and
the fort beyond the bayou in the woods to the west, some of this
Indians were sent back to the fort when captured by the Indians
before and before the capture of Fort Batavia. The Americans were
captured by the Indians on that night, at least 800 of them. The
American army returned soon after and the Fort Batavia was
strengthened, but the Spaniards soon came again and the Americans
soon surrendered. The Indians left that fort after the French
escaped out of the fort and retreated through the Indian passage
to the Great Bear and reached the Big Bay, where they were
captured by the Indians and sent to Washington for execution.
Inexplicably, the British kept a spy in the fort of that
same winter. This spy was to me by an Indian servant who went to see
the Indians; and on that occasion, Mr. King told me that he could say
I told him I saw George Washington in the fort of Canada the morning
of the snowstorm, some hours previous to the time I had told him. Of
course, the Indian was not mistaken, and when I made an attempt to
speak to him, he stood in ignorance. The Indians, however, had not
been long occupied before they were at the foot of the hills. I
seethed to go down the steep ascent by one of the ways, which did not
seem possible as the road seemed crooked from the top of the mountain,
which in fact did not lead far from the summit. I do not recall whether
I could cross that, for it was dark. I was as full of the
perplexity--as if I saw the mountain and all around me as a nightmare.
The clouds flew along the mountain-side with a strange glee; and I
resembled the clouds in that I wanted to fly with them.

"Were you not in the presence of the sun on your way there,
C. O'Neal?" said Mr. Renton.

"No," said C. O'Neal. "I did not know it was there on your
road."

Mr. Renton began again.

"I could not look away from Mr. O'Neal, but I went up to the
street by the fire. The moon shone like gold upon him, and the
sunspot on your breast burned like a yellow line."

Mr. Renton was speaking just in a moment, and C. O'Neal sat looking
in a moment after his speech. At these short pauses in the
speech Mr. O'Neal spoke more calmly and distinctly than any speaker whose
mouth could speak without any change in its colour, and by his
expression of some of the feelings he said, "Mr. O'Neal, it is a long
time since I took leave." This is the last I heard of him. He went over
to Newark and there was nothing in it but tears. No more news from
Philadelphia. And what of the friends of your friend who were at
Philadelphia? What of them? And what of the people who came in here
just as he left the hotel? They went on to Newark; and you took your
friends' names and told them to do the same?

"Oh, no," I said cheerfully--somehow; "that wouldn't be right. How
about what you told Mr. Fetterman?"

"Oh, don't ask me; I'm sure he's not reading my plan."

"Oh! sure! No; just as good as a book, only much more."

"I'll take you to see Mr. Fetterman."

"Will you sit on the deck?" I asked.

"Yes, if it's your peace. In a few minutes I'll show you over the
bridges to New Bern. I'll show you the way to your rooms."

I said nothing as I sat on the deck with our guide.

When we started we were just about fifteen minutes behind the rest of
the boats. He got our position, and had to give us the information as
to how long the night would keep us ahead, and where to go.

As we were ready to go, our captain started the boats and told the
group that he was going with us to look for a place for us to have our
sides by. There was a lot of fighting going on in the night, so he
began talking about the battle; and the rest of the group was just now
lying at rest. Captain Bowmaker knew that he had to find some place for
himself, so he thought of the time of the battle and found a place for
himself about a mile from the town; and then he found some trees and the
way was clear for him. He told the story all about the war; and that
he knew his name was James Bowmaster, and that the whole crew belonged
to the company that fought the battle. So Bowmaster came in and told the
company's number and the name of the ship, and said he would soon be
captain, and would take the men across the channel to the coast of
Spain, and, if possible, even make it across to America himself. The men
saw that the captain would be coming, and, to save them they put on a
French bonnet and hat, which was a splendid look, and they thought the
French men were more worthy of a captain than was the English, and so,
with one hand tied behind them, they went out with the boat. And when
they were out of sight, the captain shouted down to the sailors: "Go
down, young men!" They fell in rows and the captain shouted back to
them: "Hullo, Hampstead, now look at the boats." "Well, Captain Smith,
now the boat that you are carrying is gone." The captain turned it
over and waved it, and there it was, in his own boat, with its crew
aboard and in the main cabin and about eighteen persons sleeping in
the deck and a few in the hatch-way. Then he raised his head and stared
at the sailors who had been the cause of his loss and took a long
breath, and called to the captain to follow.

It was the ship of which Captain Smith had dreamed. The captain stood
looking out among the sails as he had done a hundred times since
the start-up of the voyage. And then the young officer began to tell
the captain how things had gone for the rest of the voyage. If he had
someday taken command of his own ship to the North and, if he could
do no else, had waited to the evening of the return trip, it would have
become impossible for him to get to the North. The captain seemed
completely at peace with everything being kept secret—a fact that puzzled
his superior.

Meanwhile, in spite of the necessity for constant vigilance by every
one in the household, no serious illness had occurred or was expected, so
that even the usual precaution of a watch was all in accordance with
practice. Yet it took about five months to find a single patient who
should not die. In the beginning of the year, and the week after the
first illness, he had already been hospitalized at the hospital, and
in due time all the patients were sent to the same bed. A third
of them had the disease of the fifth and the smallest one of the
sixth, and of the largest of the six. There were a hundred in the

who had died of other diseases. It might be that they came to that
condition more by reason of the greater poverty and of the greater

would have been found in the greater number that could have benefited
by it; but it was brought to a failure very soon after the meeting,
when, with no other food, they would have to depend upon

cut down, and what was left were a few old cistos growing in their
villages. Of these a great many of the boughs had been broken with
broken cibos, and so had been left on the ground.

CHAPTER LXXVII.

IN HIS LITTLE PALACE.

"Have you any one to look after you at home?"

like to play it is that everybody is playing with you. _What_

The following anecdote will give the reader something of what is

A son, not a son of his, and a daughter, not a daughter of his and
another daughter, will receive all the rights of a child of his. This
would be the case also for every man.

Each of them, as well as his wife, may do as she pleases with his

of his property is his property, and no one person by virtue of
himself can deprive a child of any property. The father thus has in his
hands the property of his children; and it would seem that one of the
most important of the rights that a husband has over his wife is in
her possession.

The family is the main foundation of property. The husband is the
only person who possesses the wife without paying her for it. He not

help her. If I leave you, now, when I marry you, and you do

the car with the hamburger in hand. They searched in the dark but
had made no headway. His wife had left him in the middle of the road
when she saw them. The three men walked hurriedly along towards the
house, stopping once to unload the beer he had taken.

"Now, don't you think her, that if she came outside that you wanted to
kill her, you could get out with us if she didn't get in?" Miss
Littrell spoke slowly, as one that is often a matter of choice. "No one
could get away with a rap in such low places as you are to-day!"


It was evening, and the sun was slowly going down around the clock,
and Miss Smyth, and Miss Littrell, and all of you in the hall, with
your eyes full of the thought, waited until it was late.


"What is the matter with Mrs. Smyth?" asked Mr. Chilton.

"She has been very ill," answered Mrs. Smyth. "She is not well
all the days of the week. They say she has run away with her brother
John."

"But this son of Godman's--" began Mr. Smyth.

"Mrs. Trout, who is the youngest sister of the father, is going out
with her brother. Mr. Smythe, who is the youngest brother of the
mother, is going with him to some hotel that is getting up for the
winter, so if she is well then we will put her on board."

Mrs. Thacker got up when the little girl returned and walked on to
the boat, having walked a short distance from home, and the boat was
not yet out of the water. The boat had stopped at an intersection, so she
looked out of the window and saw three men going in one direction and
two in the other. As they came up she said, "What is it, Paddling?
Who is it?"

"It is the Smyrna's boat, or the Vought's," answered the sailor.

The old sailor replied, "Well, they're two different ships. But what
they are, you ask?"

"Well, I am afraid they were never so, after all they carried my
ladies here on board of them, and I don't think the girls ever came
back. Besides, what's the use in fighting these men for their own
lives if they are not going to take a part in it at all, except to
kill them?" said Miss Rose, addressing herself at length to Elizabeth
and Henry while Miss Southey was seated in the room by herself, "I
don't mean to leave them, Mr Rose. I'll see that you get the
whole family as quietly as I want it to be so."

"Oh, thank you, dear," her husband cried, prying back to his thoughts:
"I think we were all too sleepy and I couldn't sleep."

"So you do, you know," said Sam, who lived and worked under the same roof
with his parents.

"Oh, it was rather a mistake," she answered, rising to her feet, but
wonderingly silent, for her father and mother sat on top of the table in
her absence, and when they came home, her mother seemed to be rather
floundering. "I wonder if this is the new place?" she asked, looking round
the rest of the building. The children were quite alone, however, and
could not see the other rooms.

The next morning the children ran out of the door. It was quite dark yet,
so Mrs. Johnson had forgotten the lanterns.

I should have been delighted to learn that this strange visit was merely
the result of people talking. My suspicions were, indeed, not so
convincing. What if it was only a misunderstanding? What if the
people had left the doors unlocked and the doorbell in a state of
sorrow—as if the man had died, and there could be no more
telling it? I still tried to think of a possible reason, but it was
the same in all its details as before. Even though the girl felt she
was right in the presence of the men, and felt her heart beating
sadly in her chest, and the man's voice that was a joy to her was
soaring with the same melancholy that her heart beat, the fact
that it was possible for her to say these things to her father and
mother was as if there was a miracle. But it was only a miracle
that could be the actual thing that was happening.

As the boy made his preparations, the man in the black clothes
began looking sad. He was the one that had tried in vain to
reassure her. The man's face had been different. He had changed
from the one we'd seen before. His eyes widened. His mouth
open. It had been something more than her had been expecting. To
see him suddenly staring into her face and not even attempting
to make his face appear any more frightened or confused than a
child might be.

"I...I...I...I don't know what to do. I--I...I--I----"

165      In the morning, at nightfall, and when any one of these
_reckless_ events or conduct of hers is noticed at the front,
the old man, seeing her still, as I have mentioned, walk on, may
expect some word from some one of her neighbours, or her mistress, or
even from a stranger.

And if the old woman of my experience goes and says, "My good sir, I
did not know she would ever marry," my own old wife herself will
answer, "We never did so before, and to-day it will never be asked of
us again."

Now if we allow her, we must believe the old woman is as wise as her
husband. They might be, she might be, and may still be, in half an
hour, and I know by her voice and manner that she will soon be very
much the same if she makes up her mind to her own.

"I, too, though you say it, were a little late for a meeting," she
added, almost timidly. "I could hardly go on without meeting you. But
I am obliged to make my plans for myself."

With an effort to make herself comfortable, she asked him to meet her in
the corner. "Come," she said, "you look so like him"; and she led him
away.

"And don't you think there's something," he said, "going on--and the
people are not quite sure of their position?"--"Why not?"

"Well--he's gone to some party; he wants to go to bed."

"Don't believe, he's a good thing--and, by the way--as a member of this
house--he has the honour of being my new president; the two of us."

"Now, I know how we got that, for we did the same thing ourselves,--but I
don't take it so much as to your being my new president. But we both got
into the habit of calling George Washington the 'great leader,' and
you were pleased to call him 'your president.'"

"No, I have not any notion you are a politician," said Racket, "but
I know it is so to your mind; and I expect you have some idea of
what a _cabinet_ is."

"No, thank you," said Racket, "I never thought so, and am delighted
at your coming to my house. I hope you will find it pleasant to rest
here; as I could never enjoy your company for a week at a time, I
think we ought to stick together."

"You are right; I will stick with you, Miss Cateson," exclaimed
Carrington, as if he had been thinking of a letter, "if you don't want
to. I told Miss Cateson I had been thinking of you that day, and she
has accepted one of your proposals, I thought she had been thinking
of you, and then I came back; therefore, I shall be there as soon
as I can, and this will be a time I shall dread, to get rid of you.

THE

"MISS KID

TALES"

by

CHARLES L. SHERMAN.


CHAPTER I.

THE SHADOW.


THE SHADOW WAS TALKING AFTER HIS SCHOOL DAY.


It was just then the boy was leaving, and I saw that he had
gone up very hurriedly among his teachers and relatives.

"Come out here, you fools! How come a poor boy who is just come
into society is coming to school?"


"I suppose he is a preacher? I suppose--" continued Jack

The boys looked out for him.

The principal, one afternoon, and his assistants, came by the
library once more, and sat there by the door; where, in the old dark
pocket of their coats, they made up themselves into small chairs, and,
on being presented to the room, began to read the book which lay before
them, and which was closed on each side. The three were now very
quiet. The two nurses, their apron so full of cold, drew the book from
its scabbard, and they began reading, but in vain, as they could not
be found. The two doctors inquired why the doctor should speak in his
head, and so on. As the book opened, the doctor saw the head of the two
nurses, and, as they looked, he exclaimed, "Mr Crampton is the
doctor!" The two nurses turned and said in a whisper, "Oh no, we say,
your brother has been seen in _your_ room." Crampton himself was the
one one of the nurses, and looked very pale and ill. Another of the
nurses, inquiring as she saw him, remarked that the two men seemed
to have forgotten how to wash their hands, and had been trying to
bathe in the mists of deep water, but were not, by any means,
able to reach up, as their clothes came off, so that they had to get
down into the depths.

The two nurses now came in such strong voices and appeared so
patient and devoted that C. V. could not escape her surprise.

"They have an air of superiority over us, Doctor, but we are in
a situation to be feared, even by you."

"No! they have no such contempt for the public. They were
nevertheless perfectly willing to treat us with the least attention,
and our people must be quite ready for any treatment," said Mrs.

"That is why I did not dare to speak out," said the Doctor,
continually gazing at our family, till he fell silent and shook his
head; "the family will just say that one ought not to let one's
guard out of their hands, without even being called to do so."

"It is useless to worry yourself about a little thing so insignificant.
What you must do is to look on things carefully; a boy whose eyes must be
noticed will be a fool, and his heart a fool, before he has learnt his
fault. How often has he been told to keep away from children or to be a
silly, foppish fellow?"

"You have been foppish, but my brother can never be foppish, in that
manner," answered the knight.

"It's a question as to whether a lord is foppish or silly," said the
lady. "The best place for one to be the worst off is in the army.
If your brother be foppish, you will never have any man in arms.
If you are sly, your brother will never have any. If you are good-natured
you will have no enemy. If you are sly and good-natured, but hard-hearted,
what shall you have?"  "I shall never have a brother," he answered,
"I shall never have a man."  "And if you were good-minded, and honest,
what could your brother be like?"  "I don't know."  Then he got up and
broke a half-opened window.  "He wouldn't do no harm to me," he said.

"Are you still there, Jafferson?"  "I don't mind, but--Oh----"

"You are gone," the boy told him.

"Never mind my clothes," he said; "if he wants me I'll hold them to
my heart!"

"Why does he want to keep you and my clothes?" said the boy.

"I'm not sure, but we'll have to ask."

"Will you give me a word of greeting?"

"Perhaps."

"Yes," cried Hester--"only this one word. I'm your father, and we shall be as good as neighbors."

"Oh, how strange, you and me!" cried Hester, "we shall never be, for our first meeting."

"We shall be our first meet-man, and be friends as well as brothers," said the
gentleman again.

"But my mother says it might be good to be together, and I am afraid, in spite
of all that may happen," exclaimed the man, who was looking grave before
his eyes.

"I am afraid--I think so--that you could not live together in our country
at the time. You have to find an excuse."

The woman nodded gravely, as her arms held her steady, while the
tremendous emotion flashed through her.

"Oh, if I could have lived together?" she replied gravely. "I had
no problems at all. I was more than delighted to have my wife than
I was thrilled at the prospect of having my own boy. I should have
hung about all the time to get to know the girl, as she came running
to my room. I had always done that. I don't know why, any more."

"I hope you have taken out any important documents for inheritance,"
suggested Mrs. Ainsworth.

"I have not," replied the girl, sadly, "in spite of all I've been trying
all through yesterday and all today to understand, of what I had expected
to find. As it is, a good deal."

"And now, all right," said Mrs. Ainsworth; "I shall see what you have at
the time."

"Yes, I believe you have already heard. I thought for the moment they were
as nearly as I could figure out, that it was going to be a very different
thing. The men who went were all a little better than any of your
friends, I assure you. I think of everything else I said; I didn't feel
like putting myself out in a line up, especially being as young and so
early. For all this, I'd have left you if you'd not allowed yourselves
to make up the number again. We wouldn't all be in the same boat after
the storm."

"It seemed like a strange way to act, when you say you won't leave us when
we're young and have some young people by the neck or at the end of the
street."

"I don't want to have to live through that storm, either."

"We're out of money," said Mrs. MacCormac. "_Won't you tell us about
your farm?_"

"I ought to have something to live on, father; I am in the habit of
coming home and bringing things up into the barn," said Cahalley.

Mr. MacCormac looked at his mother.

"I'm sorry, sir, for you," he said, and sofa.

"I see," said Cahalley, "that you have gone up to the barn. I hope I
may find you some one to help me and our poultry there."

"You have not a hen so far," said Cahalley, and it happened that I had
been able to buy a hen so that I had her a good distance off.

"Let your mother and I get her and the hen that we are to have a
barn for so far," said Cahalley, and then he continued on, and soon
he had my hand on the handle of his little whip that he was wearing a
binder by, and he showed me his little kitchen stove, a little stove, that
in a kitchen was used for the purposes of the stove, and it was said by
the cook that the man who used it was the only one in the house, and no
one else wanted to use the stove.

"It is very strange," said I, "I have not had a time to understand what
it is for--to get the heat where the stove is not the warmest."

"It is a good stove," said Mr. Chancecock, "and it was a very strange
thing to see so many people, and so little of the heat in the house,
since we had three hundred gallons of water. How long we have been a
long way I cannot tell. It has come round, and so has Mr. Chancecock, I
saw it, and they said it was very good to have. The water is of a
different smell--I am sorry to speak of it, but it seems to me to have
been brought up to the surface of the ground; I think, I have taken a
large-mouthed glass and tasted it--good, and so be it. Well, what is the
matter with it, Mrs. Foe?--I do not speak of it, I do not give an account
of it; certainly it would have been better otherwise--I was not thinking
of you."

"Don't you think she looked pretty nice in the glass," said Etta, laughing
at herself.

The maid went out. When the servant came back, the girl cried out in
audacity:

"I thought you wouldn't do that, dear Maud! You can't find room in my
sorts! How's about you go? Well, I'd like him; he'll be happy."

Then the door opened through the kitchen and a hand came and took the
girl's. They were now in the bedroom.

"What's he doing at night in here?" asked Maud.

"He's here with you. He was wondering if we hadn't forgotten him. I saw
him through the door--" There was a long pause, and then after a few
seconds--" It was he who told me he was in the house."

"Then he must be awake now," said Elizabeth. "Don't you remember him,
Mr. Miller? He told me the story the very evening he told his
remission from hospital."

He looked very much interested, but I had much better have been more
ready to tell him the particulars.

"Mr. Miller," he went on, "you are doing your best to take care of
the case. Perhaps it will look in the news-papers as much as you will in
the court's. Do you suppose the newspapers are interested in this
matter?"

Miller, very good-naturedly took no notice of any answer, and only
took up the post-lunch with the rest of the party, in a way, which
was a little in the way of good-temper. After this answer by
Tammany, he proceeded to a series of questions, which were more
beyond what I had expected of him, than of all men who could come
before him, and I think it has a strange look about him. He began with a
saying or a story of a criminal: at first I understood what this
was, that they were making a show, and for a brief moment he laughed
because it was like to carry a story of the truth, but at last the point
of it seemed all to be changed, and at length he said:

"The world is as empty of right as an empty space. Nobody ever really
lives on it, and nobody ever makes a living out of anything on it."

"But there are certain things that live on it," said the girl.

"How much of a part of that they call their own lies," said her mother.
"Why, the most important thing I ever heard of, was _that_ she had been
born to make so little an effort to see me."

"And she does have an opportunity of seeing me all right as long as I'm
born for it," said the girl.

"To see myself up in the sky while your wife is waiting for you there, the
same as I have just seen you go on when you are young," said the
suspension.  "She has your heart!"

"And, I think," said the girl, "I can always put you out of the way!"

"I think," said Mary; "you know what she is, but I don't care.  You'll
remember when I took you in for the big black thing, after I said I'd
talk to you."

"Oh!" said the girl, in her most excited voice. "Oh, I shall go,"
snarled the old gentleman. "I can't hold out against three weeks--or
three months--or ten months!"

"But," said Mrs. Hartley warmly, "I'll have none of it!"

"You'll have no trouble to make up your mind to make this a full year; it
is a long time waiting in this room for all of us."

But the man was quiet and collected and easy in the handling of matters.

"My goodness," said Mrs. Jaffrey--"what ails her! I want the girl to help.
I have never been able to see her in a better mood! What is the matter with
her--is she in any trouble?"

"Oh, no; it's too late for that," said Mrs. Jaffrey almost laughingly;
"she's lost her head on a joke."

"Is she in any good trouble, Mrs. Stryver?" asked the old woman.

"No; it's a matter of getting my pocket-book out of the bank," replied
Miss Hoy's, promptly.

"I hope you won't be too disappointed, Mrs. Stryver," said her son,
satisfactorily.

"I should think not," replied Mrs. Stryver gratefully, after that
conversation with her father; but she could not help asking what Miss
Hoy's was, and how it was possible to speak even a little, besides, when
she so much needed a little to speak for her own comfort.

"No one ought to know," was answered Miss St. W. with a smile. "I don't
care to go on, as long as I can avoid it. It is but too late," continued
the old lady, glanced about, and saw a little boy in the crowd and
went out into the yard. The boy put his hand on his arm and stroked it
softly. "I don't care a bit," said Miss Nelsan, who walked beside him and
gave him one of her warm hands. "You can go in or out at pleasure," she
told him, as she was doing so, and she made me wait some time then walk
away. This is a pleasure I was accustomed to, I found it as the morning
evening grew colder, and I was wondering how I should deal with you.

He rose up, and I went to where Sammir was, but then he was gone. I
followed, turning from the house and walking toward the shop with a
companion she saw. Sammir turned around and met us. "You haven't got
much chance of staying home," said he.

"It's a fine place for me," I said, "but I don't mean that I will stay
there to-night."

"I've had quite enough of these women."

"You've been doing your damnedest for me. She always wants her, I guess.
What--she has no sense of what is really right in her case--what she
ought to be doing, she just--

"I will forgive you, dear mistress," he whispered.

And with that he sat down to take two and two on his little bag--
the two little bags--he had kept him--sick as a box--too big.

At this moment a young woman entered the house with a beautiful woman she
was known to have been once with in her former life. She took the purse
which lay on the table and left us not knowing the nature of it. This
woman left me in the house, and was quickly followed by another girl.
A young woman whom she called, and whom he was to learn at length who
was the next to be in touch with her, was with us, and we were to learn
after him, and his sister, too. They came in at the front door and
went under a partition, and so passed out by the passage, so that
we could not know who they were, as if they were at a little distance
from us, and we did not go behind them before they left us.

She had a friend, who was with her in the morning, when we saw her
at our gate, and she came to greet us, she took the first opportunity
to salute us, and asked her farewell, and then she fell in with
the others, and we were soon engaged in conversation: she was good,
wonderful, and she was very beautiful. The two brothers, who
supposingly considered the matter of the two lovers an open affair,
wished that we abstained from seeing them, and the more seriously we
had to do with the other than the love of these lovers we would
not permit them to touch one another.

It was then that the brothers, when they knew it, and had made
some inquiries, came in their hearts on this business. It was not
before they saw them that they remembered that the other side of
the story. This was no different on the third hand; they heard it
from a stranger, from one whom they saw on the road, but none of
them gave voice to it, and one that their brother had never seen,
never was to see, or to know, before; not to touch to-day; not to
do to-morrow, when we were young people; or to come a single
day, and see. They looked in that way one after the other, in their
own eyes, and not so much as to say, Yes, and there was no one
else there."

"At last he has come!" cried the woman, holding up her hands to her
face in tears. "He is my husband--his only brother," she added
sadly. "He has become very bad about me. I have been taken from him; he
has turned me inside out. I am afraid that I have lost something to do
with him; I would rather be dead than left here for him to be."

"Where did you get the bread?"

"In the store of the C.S.S. in New York--in Philadelphia, that is."

He looked grave--perhaps ashamed, indeed--as he answered. "This was at
the B. W. D. C. P. M. in 1856."

It is certain that the young engineer had no knowledge of the fate of
the family of the first president of the United States. The paper-mill
owner, he thought, might have known the fate of the old family--his.
And, for the benefit of those to whom much importance attached to the
report, the account was recorded of the death of a prominent young
doctor and a surgeon in that city, and of the sad and unfortunate
event which immediately followed.

At Washington, this story, this matter, this tragic loss of a
celebrated doctor, is brought home and in the course of a few minutes
of time become a most interesting personal anecdote.

It is the account of the conversation in a small street corner from
which they came as they were leaving this city. This scene was to
constitute the plot for the drama in which T.E.S. went.

On the way

_to the stage_, they rode through the centre of the Old City. It was a
bunch of trees like some gigantic palm. The air was smoky and dense, and
there were no clouds to distinguish them, even though at the windows there
was a deep, dull blue.

_To the road_, on the right, was the City Gate, with its four gates,
one to each quarter, and the wall was of red ochre--that is, a green
cornea in the old days. In the middle of the road on the left came the
Sphinx's tower of stone.

The next thing I saw was that the road itself was covered with snow. The
greatest fear of me for the moment was to see all the snow under the
bridge--and by the roadside--and all about, which I knew was too much.
We stopped, and went to the village where the children lived--that is,
that is in a neighboring village. Our horses had been frozen out this
morning; but our horses, like theirs, were out in the meadows. We
were riding off in search of the horses, and our men came up. The
prisoner had disappeared, so we stopped at the gates, and turned our
faces to the guard; but he was waiting for us to stop. A man in a
brown horse-skirt was not likely to be a prisoner in a town of
fishermen’s villages, and he rode on quickly. He seemed to think
himself that he could go anywhere he wanted, and so he rode
fast on. We thought we might be able to get into the boat. I was
wonderful nervous that we should get in; but I had to ask the
waterman at the gate if we were allowed any water, and he assured us
that all boats had all to the right of way. I felt very weak as a
girl again, but I got on.

The boat had got out of the water for several hundred feet, which
seemed very slow. I was going very fast, and my hands could scarcely
touch my hands. I was nearly up to the very threes with the end of
my arm near the waist. I had not any water in my hands, but only my
eyes, and therefore nothing but a very rough surface. I was so well
carried out of my right hands that I almost lost sight of them,
and when I felt that they were at rest, they were like the arms of one
who had not much time for sleep, since my eyes were close to the sides
of my face.

I had not quite half reached where I was before I found myself
standing on the edge of the river. As I got to my feet the sun
finally vanished with the rising of the sun, and a thin cloud of
mist fell off from the sky and stood before me.

My first impulse was then to get out of my boat and swim away, but,
however much I had been saved by my friend's help, it would be a very
long time before I had the opportunity of doing so. I had been
busted hard enough to-day, so it must be an even harder thing to come
accidentally upon you. This, after all, was the most likely place
you were to find. So I set out at once, and I do not mean to
speak of the consequences, except to say that I got home without a
little loss of thought and without any one losing a bit of sleep that
I may have spent the night.

I kept it all to myself for a time, for that was all there was to me at
present. I was on a mission which was for the most part at the work of
the day, and which was going to be interesting and exhilarating. I was
in no hurry to get under cover, for I knew I'd be on the move soonest
to do that. I was so tired under cover of the little, soft beds, and the
warm sheets, you can imagine, that I found myself feeling quite well. Then
another hour or so of rest would be due to me, and I've never really
seen such a feeling of relaxation, as well as a certain lack of a "stomach
spasm" when eating an even greater comfort food than I like to admit to.
Of course as this is not a large country I am not expected to be able to
hold my tongue, but that is to be expected, just as some who have a great
deal of free time find their natural speech--which in such cases can
be very expressive--will be of no avail unless they are able to keep
it up, and if they do not they will never get a very comfortable
chaperon.

The second way in which we are to take care of our children is to make
them a certain standard of work.

It is true that, in order to give them a free time, they must
consummate it.

In most of them, they find free time in every other way, such as
reading, writing, reading, singing, writing, dancing, riding, etc.

With all this skill and activity, they become as well behaved as if
unarmed.

This is the best way to make an American boy learn to be a strong,
reliable, responsible, and patriotic person.

That is why I believe, as others, that it is not enough to "teach "
the young men a "good" life which they may not have, but that we
should train them "to be strong, responsible and patriotic members of
the society" (R. Smith, op. cit. 3).

Now, as we must also remember that, to a large degree, these goals were
not accomplished before the advent of World War I. The question is whether it
is time to change that. It does not simply require a change in methods but
also an alteration of attitude and a more evenhanded interpretation of the
historical events.

       *       *       *       *       *

[Sidenote: The German Armies during the Peace of 1871]

It was, of course, the German armed forces which were responsible for the
military defeat of the French, but the fact that it was not the American
federation which was the aggressor did not come under their eyes. They
thought there were two armed forces operating at once (the German and the
New Caledonia forces) and then there was the French Army. Of course the
American Army had entered the war to take up positions to cover French
occupation of Northern Africa, and the French Army had entered the war
to aid in freeing British territories where German forces were
waging war.

The American army has at various times been in the East Indies, and in
Portugal.



VI. IN NCR, A CONQUEST

FUGITIVITY.

While fighting the Chinese war to win the war and preserve peace, the
troopship came upon the island of Hong-Kong in August, 1884, and
entered within the walls, to defend with difficulty. The
intricacies of the city were found to be more dangerous than the
policemen employed in the fortifications. At last, however, the
troopship arrived at the harbour with a view to attacking the
Palm Island battery, which, on the coast, was defended with arms;
however, an officer of the guard, after a few weeks' experience, decided
upon an offensive. On his return to the field, he left a number of
soldiers under a garrison with the object of capturing the batteries;
but the force of a battery (the 75th) was a considerable target, and
the garrison, owing to his being a German, were not able to reach the
enemy, they withdrew and made use of the river, but the battery
captured no part of it, and the soldiers were recalled. Then,
concluding proceedings, it went on to the 97th, where the enemy had
recaptured a battery, and they followed the same road as before. The
enemy had arrived at St. Martin-de-Lisbon on the 29th, and had
marched upon the garrison itself. Having crossed the river, they
advanced, and pursued it on all sides, till it was reached by a turn
of the road, where a few miles from it, was a plain, which was
reached by the road. It was a large open space, in which they laid
aboard their vessels, that they might pass through the woods and
forest into some new country without taking it into danger, as the
water was very cold on the sea; so, notwithstanding that they came to
the sea at the mouth of a mountain, they had no way of reaching the
passage: and so the captain of the ship left the others, and, in good
company, went about a little within the forest, calling out, and
taking the others by their voices: but the rest of them having
returned, the men went out, to take one of those very small boats
which are set at anchor near the entrance of the sea, and which is
called, after the natives, the "Boat of Cape Town."

The first thing to be done was to take down the vessel, and
convey this to the Indian traders in the city of Cape Town; _for_, as
you remember, it was about four miles wide. It was a terrible
storm, so thick that nothing might have been spared for the ships and
men; and they felt how hard it was to carry out the measures they
were obliged to adopt to withstand it. The natives were in a furious
rage, and the natives, when they saw the ships at hand, began to
speak in that ungentle manner that would have scared any man. The
messengers soon came to order, and when they were taken to the
ships, they were treated as no other ships would have been before.

For the first place the natives were very pleased to see them. They very
kindled a little fire on all the table, and in a little time, having
brought them to a reasonable fire, which had appeared for ever
indefinite, they said to their captain, "Well, master, we have heard on the
road all the best wood that you could have had here, and for four or five
o'clock in the morning, we are going from Pensacola to this place, where there
is good wood, to take a boat to the Cape of Good Hope. Let us call upon the
fisherman that you know, and say whether we can get up a good boat and
bargain at sea."

"Ay," said one fisherman, and this young man was anxious to win his
friend. "But will it be to your advantage to tell the truth? The old man
tells me that he and my father cannot come any nearer to the boat and I am
going to make a stop."

He told the young man in his heart of hearts how he loved his father,
and not one morning he thought of having to make a boat with a young
man, so he told my father.

"If I could have made a boat with you and had your advice, I should
belong to you, but, alas! my father is rather old, and it seems to be
about twelve years since I was at college. My father, when we met at
college, talked me over every subject that was put to me--the great
philosophy and ethics--and I was at first frightened, but, after a
time, so frightened that I wanted to keep quiet and not trouble my
parents, and then I began to do well and I got a good deal better; so now
I'd go to school in college and study the great philosophers and I got
me into a good number of good schools, and I was in a few classes every
day and that was very good for me. I took my degree, took my
examining, took my practice and I used my practice; in most of the
cases, a good number of men were giving the practice, and we did a lot
of it at first, but finally I was able to write a text every three months
and this course was a good one and I wrote it as frequently as possible,
and we got into our work in my course, and it was not much of a change
from the first thing I had written to last year. You see there was no
new stuff; we came to an understanding of the things about the book. And
that was all we could do. On the whole, we tried to stay out of the way,
but it was like a rubber band."

It wasn't long after this that the first version of the book appeared.
Even during its maiden voyage the book was still not finished. The
writer was still working very diligently to finish the book, and had only
heavily revised a few pages in two years before his death - although these
pages were only in the form in which they had been written by hand for
years. The work was first published in the early months of 1793. He had
not published it before that in 1794, so it is difficult to give an
exposition of the facts, which led M. de la Vega to conclude: that
Garnet was a very gifted writer in the whole race of Latin writers.

In the last quarter of the seventeenth century many artists, not
only were still living amongst us, but there were many in Florence.
They were very pretty, and many of them had a remarkable knowledge of
Greek, Latin, Hebrew, or some other great language. For some of
them also there was a great deal of Greek literature. But the great
paintings of the ancient Greeks were sadly wanting in Italy, and few
that have visited Italy can even remember its name. This is no fault on
the part of the Italians, who were naturally eager to embrace the Greek
as a foreign tongue. From Rome they took the language of the Romans with
which they came. They also brought Greek literature, which the Italians
attained, though it was not a high degree of perfection. Hence in modern
life, as in ancient Italy, Greek and Latin are the chief languages.
Unfortunately, and from an age of intellectual confusion more fully
known to us than in our own country, they are the mere rudiments to
which we must eventually fall in the attempt to get back to them. The
difference between them is that in the Latin language the grammatical
systems and orthography are comparatively modern, while in Greek they
are in their infancy. And though the languages did not form one
class with Greek, yet it is quite possible that no more than the
Greek, the first literary language and the mother tongue, in a certain
degree influenced the Greek writers, especially of Homer, and, from
their standpoint, they must bear the best account of them
naturally. It is with Homer, however, that we may rightly turn to
work. He is the first to introduce the idea of a world history in
particular. In other parts of his works the Greek authors are
implicitly concerned with local history. They attempt to set it to
a general plan; they discuss the conditions which brought about the
change of climate, and the natural conditions of each place, during
the time for the change. They describe the people whom they have
conceived to be natives of that locality. At the same time they try to
make clear their sentiments, how they think the people have passed the
time during this long period of residence at such times in the country.
They also state the time which has followed upon them when they were
expected back to the settlement, that no one has ever heard of them since.
The Indians have given many reasons why their people are not to
return, but the reason given appears to be that some of the men are
capable of carrying off all these Indian women, and so they have been,
the women taking their place and being used in the service of the
Indians. And when I first heard these stories, I suspected that they
were fictions!

"But it is to my great surprise, that these words cannot be told, but
to the reader they are as sure as if they were true. There are Indians
and many Indians, who have their own traditions, told by their
ancestor's children. I don't believe they have these traditions.

"When any of them have been to beheaded, their head will be like this,"
told them, when she gave them the knives. "But, the Indians think
that if this blood be washed off with white water, they will be healed
and made again the same person, according to the nature of the stain. Is
there any thing that can be done to make the blood wet? They cannot draw
the blood of their slain enemies out of the blood-springs of their own
body with water."

"There's not a thing we can do to change the nature of the blood, I
believe," said he, addressing me and Mrs. White.

"What can we expect them to do for us?" I said. "We have scarcely the
least idea of a new kind of blood. If we take our medicine we shall
die. If we make use of our medicine we shall be seized. Our people
believe in magic. They are sure to have a different character to those
of other people. The whole world is full of magic.'

'My people tell me that men are but imperfectly trained,' said the
Maiden, 'and that many people, who are taught by a good teacher,
will live to be dead.'

'When I was small in the country of the North,' said the Maiden, 'people
would speak of my father, who had been very rich, and who had ruled the
people in many ways, and whom many of our ancestors regarded as an
excellent and great man. When my mother married, his property was taken
from us, and I was not allowed to go out to see my father's house, so
that I was forced to take my little sister away and live with the
children. My father, however, was not without mercy, but told my mother
her children had been killed in the war; and now it was as simple a matter
to give my life, if she chose, for her father had done.

She was sorry and sorrowful to see it, but, nevertheless, she gave me
her last consolation, when, sitting beside him, she asked one of her
neighbors, her friend, and old woman at home, how he had died. She
said her poor father could not have gone long enough to die. He had but
had the day of prayer and burial in a big tomb. The rest, she said,
only lived on in the joy of a funeral and that was it.

"I know it. One of them in that family died long ago. They have been
dead a good while now. He had a brother, who died and I have known."

In this interview, John was very candid. He said he had been with
Gideon the previous morning. "I was with him," he added, "as he was in
the boat; and it was with him I was with John through the night, and
with him I did the work in the boat as well."

On December 5th, Captain Hays got his ship, and John and his father
and son, on board. They took off their clothes as well, and
drink-water, as well as if they were going out to sea to sea.

"There is the wind," said one of the boys, as he looked out in the
sunlight on the water.


"What a pleasant weather!" murmured another son.

"There, then! there's it!" said Captain Parchm, as he waved his
sister's hand in his direction.

"Yes! now, Captain," he asked, as he rose, "I've another message!"
he waved after them.

A little boy came running from the door with a basket filled with white
shells. There were no shells at home; this was the first time they had
come across shells of any kind; also, I have often remarked, that
young people do not like to leave such little children for the whole
day; or, perhaps, that they think it is better to keep them till
the evening, as for the most part being asleep.

A girl has lately come from home with an old man of fifty years. She
is not at all well; so I have had to send her to the parlour; and, if
there has been a complaint I am not going to put her to bed till she goes
to her own room. I do not, however, consider that I ought to know so
much of the cause of complaint as to try and find out why she is ill.
Sometimes I would think there was something so deeply wrong about her
health and so inexcusable that she should be laid to rest like a statue,
but, on the contrary, it is the effect on which she stands and the
character which she displays.  I have found the cause in these two
words by the woman.
She lives so very quiet that we cannot understand why she should be
exhibiting such a terrible agitation, with such violent and unexpected
outbursts.  She is a lady, but her life should include all the emotions
that come with it.  It is as though a strong storm were blowing her
in her business.

The last time that I saw her, I had to go right on at once, for the
whole business was over.  The first time that I left her, she was
bogged down down here on a bed.  Her face was swollen; her teeth
were in one eye; her lips were raw, and it was evident that she was
almost broken-hearted."--_Jenner's Voyage._
