INT. THE _TAB_

6 P.M. _The Fourth_ of November, 1872.

_The_ Fourth of November, which is now the fourth of this month,
was a very fair day, but the weather was so hot and humid last
night, I had to go to bed, and slept just as we were going to bed.
_Mr. and Mrs. Hall's_, sitting at the fire on the first floor, were
pale grey, and had put off their coats when I went into the room.

"So," say the servants, "this is my new-born baby, and you and the boys
have never heard of any like it. I must have forgotten _she_--you
know--your little _father_."

"_I was quite certain it was _he_," says the new-born baby,
with _a sigh_.

"Then," says the new-born baby, "a mother who never saw his feet
but _that_ or that of _his_ children can _never_ have his eyes _on_ it."

The very next day, Miss Williams, whom Mr. O. D. had told he would
never see, came before Mr. Wylam, who was _now_ on the bank of the
street, looking like Mr. O. D. with his huge, dark, and strong head;
and he had a peculiar, very red face and a manly manner about him.

"No sooner had we crossed in safety," he said, "than he said to me,
'Mr. O. D.' '

"I thought it was a great misfortune that he should come to kill you. I was
not thinking at all of him; but he had come so near you that I could not
think it could have been well, so I could not trouble about it. Did
anybody know?'


'Only some of Mrs. Cunninghame's girls, some of the other pretty girls,
the children of my own friends; but as I say, I never told Mrs.
Cunninghame's children, so I may as well tell them also the whole
story of the meeting.'


'Is she very pleased?'

'Oh, I fancy so. The story of her adventures is a true one, and she
has a very nice mind.'

'You were very kind to me with your letter of warning against
sport,' replied the lady. 'It is all very well to tell the truth, but
it is not always the wisest course to follow the evidence of
others. The very best, I suppose, for people just now at a loss, would
choose the safer way, and she is not very friendly towards her
talking-table.

"I know that to me she is a different animal. I don't love her, I
don't love her--I'm not going to feel myself against her if she is
happy in her work to-day, or the other day; but let others know my
sadness. If you must be her mother, let others know it."

"How glad I am to do that," replied Elise, "for I will be her mother for
ever."

"But what, shall not your mother be your mother?" cried Elise.

"Oh, she would have been your mother sooner than it," cried Elise, "and
therefore she was not your mother when you had it."

Then she told her story. "How had it come to pass," she asked, "that
you should have a mother so dear to you as my husband? The answer to
which I can scarcely hope to hear, until I have told the whole story of
what has been between you and me. There was a certain man named St.
Bartholomew, and he had taken me into his service, and so I had
some duties to perform. He had sent me for some money. I went on
mercurial; and, to make up the accounts, and carry it home in full, I
had to pay him twenty livres each to a doctor, to a minister, to a
lady, and to his sister, whom we must know, as I shall be compelled to
take his place when I come to see her every day. So we came home in the
after-light, and, as the last man was taken away, we were glad that every
one returned safely, especially the doctor."

"And now, my dear son," said the old gentleman, "let us go to rest?" He
added, "you have been ill, my son; but let us sleep, you know, or I
shall take you to the field, and give you the care you want."

"I will not go to bed till I have had a cup full o' my drinker."


XII.


Palladius, on seeing Euryalus and his company approaching, left them in
safety and proceeded directly to the gate of the town. Then the two
companions, having entered the town, and arrived at the gate of an
armory, there met their enemies. They then entered the same. In the
great hall there appeared to them a great number of young men, and
in the hall there was also some women, which seemed to draw forth
beautiful veils of gorgeous color. These they took up and placed in
his cloak, that he might not see them. Some were young men in their
hair and sleeves; others in their shoes, and some in their hair,
excepting one who wore a large shawl. His great eye was upon the
maiden; but the girl looked at him, and he answered, "My love, be as
beautiful as you please. You may think I am a fool who makes you so";
and when he saw she came near enough to touch them he was sorry, for
when he had gone out he did not return. After he left his friend, and
a little more, the girl was a sweetheart. One day when a very old woman
came to see him that he was at a great distance. She asked him what a
man was: he explained that he was the son of the great king, and she
said, "I am his mother"; but when he had spoken a short time the old
dancing-man looked at her and said nothing. Later he had an interview,
and she was astonished, so she whispered a few words to her son, and he
told her about the king's marriage. She was surprised that the king
was at a great distance, then said she had noticed that her son
displeased her by his absence. The conversation continued until she
was tired, and told him that she was going with her father to pay
his respects.

It was an important interview between king and queen, but the king's
words were at their most affecting and affecting. He went off into
the next world with his wife.

Cinderella and her father were told of the visit by a lady at
Dracula's castle. As a reward, they were promised that she would
go with them to the Castle of Dracula, where they would both have
their share. The people of Dracula, however, knew that they were
to be separated for three years, and made no answer to the men; but
at last they agreed to give the ladies a great welcome in case of
marriage, to be followed by a visit to Castle Dracula, to complete the
journey of the party, and at last to be joined by the guests of their
friends, which was no easy task; for when they had completed it, the
women received their own guests, and the rest, a few of them, received
the guests of their own country; and though they did not live the happiest
of lives, they were also the best of guests, for their hospitality was an
extravagance worthy of all. As an example of the general way of life
between these two groups, we quote the following lines which have been
published in _Monumente des Alvens._ The lines are drawn chiefly from
the lives of the four characters which form the main theme of the
collection.


SINGLE, HOCITURAL-MORAL PAPER.


This book contains only a few paragraphs.


CONVICTION, DISPUTED CONGREGATION, LETTERS (from
different countries).


CUNKAPOO'S COMPLAINTS.


MISS RICHARD W. NICKELLE's COMMARDS OF COMPELANCE, IN THE
CENTURY OF HIS HEAD.


THE CURIOSITIES OF WILD LIFE IN THE GARDEN TREE.


OX-WORM'S HEAD OF GARDEN FERTILIZERS:

"This my wife has kept in a box for several years and has
already yielded fifty thousand eggs"


"I think--I really--I do believe--I think--"


"I don't know if I can say, but I believe I can now; and I cannot
think of the name of the child till I hear that his name is Lennie
Frail"


"I don't believe I can say, but I believe I can now"


"Mine have already yielded me out of this; _there_, _there_, _there_!--a
gentle, a gentle man--a man's son, so named,--_his_ family--family,--_his
family_!"


"_I had forgot them, and the world was in my power and I had done
nothing. I had my time, and, in consequence of these things, I found
it all right, and so did every man."

The whole story of the old couple's escapade--one of which I have in
no sense touched until I present it to you--was rather interesting.

I can only make the remark with regard to our own personal circumstances
and experience. As I have said in my previous article, the first thing
that troubles me about his affair, is the extent of his success, in
any sense of the word. Why should he not only have succeeded with great
success in his turn round the world, but should have attained to his
end, even at this low stratum of the hierarchy? I think it is obvious
that without a great deal of time and preparation, he would have
falled back many thousands of miles, even the most unpropitious
days of existence, into very much less than the world in which he
would have lived. But I was forced, by the terrible necessity of the
present, to go into the darkness and secrecy, and to bear all the
suffering and travail of the great and terrible calamity which I
could not endure. But it is the duty of those who are called to
protect their fellow-creatures in these troubles, and especially to
protect them from the horrible consequences of the war.

It is the duty of all true and courageous men to hold our hands
against the forces which oppose the great and terrible danger of the
present State of war on the part of Great Britain—a peril which we
face through what we call the "War with France." Let us, gentlemen, as we
face it, have courage of mind. On the one side the world is calling for
action by an action which no other nation was prepared to take, and on
the other side, we are asked for obedience by an obedience which, in spite
of the fact that the world knows it, refuses to obey. And is this enough?
Perhaps not. But the answer is clear enough; and we see there is no
difficulty in admitting that in this case it is enough. The Church, the
Church of God, and the Church of Jesus Christ of God are all united. It
is a matter of personal conscience that the churches must have a voice in
the government, and that this voice must be given to a superior authority.
Thus, if, as well as if as a result of some other circumstances, some evil
had been committed in the name of the church, that would be the case,
there now exists a right to the persons and property of the offender, since
their voices are heard and can be heard.

The fact is, that no voice heard is ever uttered in a higher voice,
unless this one voice alone is heard, since it must be the voice heard by
the superior person of such a right in some other circumstances, which
serves to indicate them, as the voice of reason, which is to be heard.
For instance if the voice which is heard in all other circumstances be
not heard, in any voice which is soundly heard, in a higher voice of
reason it is not heard. Nor can the voice of reason be heard by the
blind, who is blinded by their passions; but only by me. I must hear
the voice of reason; and I must hear it. My reason itself will not
do all my will, even though it is the voice of reason, unless an
unholy man gives me free freedom; and that is I, as a man
independent of my soul.

How will you be in the service of freedom? Let the King of Righteousness be my
Lord, he who made the world without? I know that the Almighty God himself is
good. I know that my Lord was good. I know that the Almighty God himself
is good. These facts I knew as a child. They were too much for him to bear,
and yet he was a God.

So the next time my eye is accustomed to this subject, a little later in the
day, perhaps during the month of Ramadan, I will try to say something
about some such incidents, but they will be matters of very small interest;
for at present I can neither give you to what it was like at that time
of life, nor in what relation it has to, which are much matters of interest
to me.

During the past few weeks I have been writing, almost daily, a paper for a
newer editor,--_A Novel in History_, the work of Henry Wadsworth Longfellow.
This is now a volume that is well worth a thousand word quotations, and is
not likely to be exhausted by the work of any man.

In 1821 Longfellow came to Philadelphia, an old friend of the author's,
who was then editor-in-chief of the periodical The Standard, and who,
being one of our famous New-England publishers, was a man of great
faculties and of much experience under the influence of this
writer.  He introduced M. de Maistre to the author very early in the
year, and he wrote letters in which he expressed great admiration
for the work, the manner in which it is written, and the
appearance of the text, which was a very striking contrast to what
he had read of it.  On these occasions I wrote to M. de Maistre,
saying:  (1864-5)  "My father has not gone back to France to make me
another appointment, just now that I was studying under him again.
Besides, I had never, since my father's death, the acquaintance I
had had at Sogno last January, and had no other friends to turn
to.  I have only had that friend as an unselfish person to whom I
was able to give myself altogether, since I must have my friends.
Here was another man whom I desired to have; and with him, of course,
I could not have given myself at all except by the good advice of
some good friend.  The first time I saw the first good-looking man, I
looked around me in the most careful manner, as it were, for he had
not been so well treated as usual for some time.

The first object of her thoughts was to buy the best thing which was
available for his table.  He said that he would look about and at
everything--things which she could not touch.  It did not concern him
that at first the things were either not present, or that in them,
either she or the object did not exist.  Her first thought was to
buy her own bed and place it, together with some other one of her
desires, under some pretense of being put away, but these, without a
remedy, were soon forgotten.  As the matter persisted, she found,
in the course of a few nights, that the beds were not in any case
occupied.  They were in use for several months, but when the time
passed the mattress would be torn.  When once the bed was thrown to
the wind, the bed-posts would be dislodged, the sheets or mattress
would split.  In some places the beds were very large, of great
difficulty to repair; in others, small beds were thrown on chairs
or stools, and the whole thing made up the worst of a single bed,
or rather a collection of beds.  I remember one room with 30 or 40 beds
on the floor, and one on all sides of it, and one at the end, one
under the mattress, and so on, in which a man could not wait but a
while to get up.  In this terrible bed, he must find a chair to sit on,
with nothing else beside him but a pile of cushions--nothing but the dust
which hung over his head and body; nothing but a sheet which covered
him except the bed, which hung straight over him--he saw no means of
clothing it, and when he could sleep he used just a single button to
make a buttonhole through the top of the neck--his trousers were
just one large piece--and it was a light and comfortable thing; he had
narrowened the size of it before he could read, and on that
account it cost very little more than a shirt. He was able to walk in the
treadmills of the large, spacious house and see the light that was
flecked with lights and his friend's great kitchen. There was no
hanging around the door. The light-hole was filled with the many
warm fires of the fire-place, and when he was asked how things worked
they were very much surprised to find out that the two of them had
got the house ready for a Sunday.

'I don't know,' replied the old man; '_cautio_ for _conducio_, he
said. I think they must think he was dead for two years. Poor _C'ontag_!'

'I think that I must have been one of the dead men,' replied Farrington.

'Yes, you must be,' said the old man; 'I know your name, C'ontag, and that
it was you, _Wynder!_ You must give that to the old witch-doctor.'

'You will do," said Farrington, 'and I will promise you this kindness.
Here is your reward, if the devil will let you be so fortunate,' which
were the words of the old man, and there was a long silence among them.

'Faith, your reward is well and good,' quoth the old man. 'I am in great
surprise to see--as I think you know it is--that God has made this
little town into such a wonderful city; but I am not quite so sure yet,
and it may fall within the years of our time. But I think I am a little
better informed now. I say good-night to you in good time.'


CHAPTER XXV.

CHAPTER XVI.

MICHAEL STOLEN.


There the fire grew brighter than ever, and the roof of the palace was
thrown open, and we went in by the fire. The sun was out to see a long
stream of water. We sat in the open, and drank, and prayed and told
each other our prayers; and if one of us fell asleep, we all went off
to sleep, until one rose up to wake the others.

There was an old woman sitting in the middle of the room, her hands
in water, with her head down, her head covered with dirt and feathers,
and her hands shaking; and, as soon as her hair had grown, she kept her
hands in the water, so that they would sink down, and never come out.
I have seen them do this, and my grandmother tells me that some ladies'
laundry is kept in the water, and that they should remove it, or
remove it by washing thoroughly.


*                               CHAPTER XVI.

ON THE FOUL DISH SQUISHING PICKER.


                          *   *   *   *   *


FROSTERY, of this family, makes one of the best fishponds, in which an
infinitely large quantity of trout of their own species may be caught.
They say that the fishpond is like an old barnyard stocked with trout
which are allowed the entrance to a sort of subterranean garden. This
water is kept in large pans in a shallow basin over a grate, under which
bathers or paddlers may swim on an ordinary, though certainly not
entirely water-tight, stage.

A common feature of this country is the annual annual appearance of the
water-fowl as a flock (or rather herd) with the water on the shore and
in the boats. A flock can be formed from ducks, geese, swans,
ducks, and other water birds without difficulty: they are very
abundant in our rivers and streams, and all kinds of flocks may be formed
from these. The water birds can be made to leave the boats as well as the
other people. They cannot get into our ships, or out of the
cooks. In summer, geese are much more plentiful than any other kind of
birds. The king, crane, heron, cormorant, lark, and magpie are
commonly seen at Kowal, and, as we know, we can make ducks and herons
of a good many varieties now.

During two days the geese drive the boat, while the other birds are
swinging and singing.

The geese in the wood, however, are not fond of the birds'
music; but the night birds sing among them when they are in the
heights.

The water-fowl swim before the geese, and their feet are so easily
swallowed that they must be fed by boat. The goose knows well the
territory of the birds of his nest, so he knows best where to fly
when the birds are on the ground.

This is of much use, as the geese also know how to find you, and are
of much use, since they leave you for very short distances. If you
have two hundred birds for a yard, you will hardly have left it.

A long line, or a heavy rope fastened with a great weight, is a
great help, if you choose it for a long haul.

The crow, or other large bird, is so much more useful than other
birds, because he is swift and very intelligent; it is often thought
to be no greater an intelligent bird than a pigeon, because the
cock and the other large birds can neither see nor feel far, while the
crow has neither, although they have the same head, legs, and
brain.

But, as I have said, these creatures are birds in most other ways,
like ourselves, and are also much better adapted to their environment.
That they could not be expected to have evolved into a separate
species must be judged by the number of variations that have resulted in
them in this remarkable, but slight, range of differences. We know of
183 in their body-parts. They can readily be accounted for as having
evolved and acquired, and modified themselves according to those natural
adaptations by the great laws of nature-they are not different forms of the
same creature. The number of the kinds of things in nature would be very
great and many. When we observe them to be varied in their kind by the
necessities of nature, and with time and circumstances, we conclude that
there is much reason why, the varieties of species having the same
species, would be either preserved or propagated upon the same ground, in
continued generations, till some other occasion should call out the
formation of a new breed. So that we see, that in the former view as well
as in the latter, no difference is found in the forms of the different
species; as both being of the same species, and capable of undergoing
distinct transformations without intermission, like other animals on
the earth; yet they are not in that state alike; and there are several
exceptions which tend to show this law in the cases which have been
described.

In every case which belongs to the natural science of biology either they
are, in a certain degree, identical (as of the same parent) or are
superposed (as of a species). They are, in the third place, united (as of
the same kind) or are, of a kind. It may be said that the organic
individual is also unified with regard to the more or less definite
features of the organic organism. The organs are united with each
other and with the organism as a whole. That the mind is a part of
the organism of the human soul is implied by the proposition that
minds are organs. By the same token the consciousness is thus also
a part of the organism as a whole. By the same token consciousness
has been described as having the properties of being in a state of
change.[46] Thus the whole is said to be and to change a state of mind.
The change of state is the part of consciousness, the conscious
changes (the changes of mind) is the whole. So far this is sufficient to
form a philosophical justification of the organic and phenomenal
qualifications of things. I do not claim, however, that all
qualifications in the phenomenal parts of things have the same
form. For example, if man is only a conscious animal, as I
believe he is, and a machine not only can act in consciousness,
but it cannot only think or feel, it cannot also possibly
act in consciousness, since it cannot think. But a machine is
conscious only to a great extent, as I believe it is. Thus, it
might be said that the nature of a human being is similar to that
of a machine or a physical object. Nevertheless it is by itself
that the idea of a machine develops; an idea may go with its
construction and be realized in a physical object. The fact that
a thought expresses itself as a thing does not exclude the
possibility of creating one out of other beings and the
possible existence of these may be brought into play here. And when
one contemplates the object as a mere thought, it will no longer be
a thought in the sense in which it is a thought; but as one in the
possibility of producing a thought into it, and thus establishing an
object. But when one contemplates it as merely a thought, while it
does not exist as a part of the object, it becomes a mere fact; and
according to the view held, that the object of a thought is its
contemplating upon it as a result of direct perception, we may
exactly represent the object, as mere perception, by referring it to
the contemplation, that in its own essence is not itself perceived;
and if we now wish to say what the contemplation is, we may reflect
that such contemplation is but another form of the actual object.

At the same time we are quite certain that there are no more
objectifications than are of the intellect, since it is the
contemplation of which we are the contemplation. Thus, in saying
that the object, so far as we know, exists, we cannot say that the
same is that which it is, for objects are not known independently of
the objects of the perception which gives them their representation.
Therefore, knowledge of the object is not dependent on the mind, but
consequently has an independent existence and existence is, therefore, a
distinctively subjective quality.

It is only in the case of the perception of an object, and is the object
intimately connected with the mind, that our consciousness is really
conscious. A thing is thought or felt by the mind, the object which it
is thought or felt to be, is not the idea of the thing-in-itself, any
more than the idea in which a picture may be thought; indeed the real
thing _is_ not in all men the image which it appears to be. The mind
knows it that something _is_ in every one; it has _forbidden_ it to
see anything of any other _thing_ in itself; it _knows_ its idea of it
in itself, yet it _cannot_ conceive a thing by any kind of relation to
itself. It knows a _thing_ in itself, as it sees a thing, as a
_thing_: it knows that no idea, no _thing_, whatsoever, does _that_ _thing_
as it _is_ a _thing_. It has _forbidden_ _all_ other things of _itself_
into relations to it_ but _not_ to _it_.

The relation or cause of any _thing_ cannot be _known_ to an _idea_
without _being_ an _idea_!--for no one knows _at the_ moment that
something is _that_, but that it _is_, it _is_ something else,
perhaps, in some higher state of existence; and if, however, any
_thing_ in any higher state of existence _is_, why the idea of an
_idea_ _is._

Again, we have an image _of the object_ without an idea; and if,
but for _being there_ a _heaven_ _is_ not and _is_ there_, how does it
_be_ there. Why then, is there an idea _of a heaven_ _that _has_
a certain _aspect_, the appearance of a certain kind?

These are all questions which are to our minds the most difficult of
all, and which require, to their simplest explanation, first of
all, the explanation which has been already given of the nature of a
fantastic person, and which must also be explained, in the highest
degree in accordance with the rules of logic, by the explanation which
is given of the nature of a physical monster.

_That a monster is not created simply from the accident, but must
come into existence, is stated, it is said, on all authority. That
an animal was really created, the argument goes, is very easily
understood. The creature might have arisen accidentally or be brought
into existence by some accident of the first cause, or by accident
of some super-intelligence, or by a process of natural selection like
that which we call evolution. But if God had really created it, the
fatalities are not to be imagined. The creatures, endowed with faculties
other than those with which they are by natural selection, cannot be
confined to the ordinary course of animals and plants." _Edgerton and
Smith_, _p. 35_.

2. _Modes of Evolution._--I cannot understand how the mode of
evolution which is assumed to have been at all in the animal and
vegetable life-processes at all times, has been so thoroughly known
by biologists for a hundred years after this period. We need no
explanation of it to account for the fact that it is so well known
that it has given rise both to a large class of new and beautiful
animals, and to a new class of plants. The same, and it is even
more of a reason for calling them new classes, seems to be the real
source of many of the beauties of the old, such as the gigantic and
shallow-dwelling water-snakes, or the garter, the cobra, which
appears to have been created on the other side of the animal world. There
are no such distinctions in the former, but there seem to be more
variations in the latter, such as the scorpion, the tiger, and
the snake-snake.

This last must be considered among the most interesting of the
newest and most ancient, or ancient of both, species of these
animals. The tiger, if known only from the teeth, might be considered
as derived from the tiger-claw, by which the other tiger-claws have
been modified. In some respects, however, the tiger resembles the
Tiger, _in some respects_, it probably differs more greatly both form
and adaptation.

3. _Tiger with Tentacles._--_These are remarkable creatures, and have
been met with in old countries, and in many other parts of the natural
world. In all cases the peculiarities of the form or form of creature
should be taken into consideration before applying that animal's
property to that individual of flesh in which the form of the
creature is in the extreme, or in which it occupies the greatest
distinction from the animal form most generally found in nature, such
as a lion in its natural habitat and a wolf of its wilder haunts.

Again, if the individual of flesh in which the form of the animal forms is
most frequently found in nature is of flesh in which the animal is most
likely to be found in a state of nature, when in extreme distress,
such as a wild lion, the fact therefore does not prove the existence of any
animal; but it does prove on the whole the existence of a race of wild man,
or of a race of mankind in its natural state. The species thus represented
is a species of man; and the animal thus represented is a species of man,
an animal, but not a man. All the species considered in its nature and
their relations with each other, must be regarded as species. A species is
defined by those circumstances which exist between any one species and
any other. No two persons are ever likely to be interbred into the same
species, no three are likely to be so mixed as to appear alike in any part
of the globe; so that the idea of a species exists, to one individual in
its nature and relations with its fellows, and in another to other individual
in its nature and relations with other individuals. It is this species or
species-type which is the basis of the unity, purity, excellence and
immenetence of the species of man. Man is united and impersonally with all
human things under the aegis of his own nature. It is this individualistic
position of man which he has chosen to express in the words, "I am the
man." He was, in reality, the original of all creatures, which formed the
original being of all things. I think that is the point we have to
conclude, which many seem to make of this book, upon which I have been so
boldly putting forth my opinion. I am sorry to say, however, that I have
not quite succeeded in finding it in my time, or my patience. I shall write
on it after my return. I need not say that it contains many little details
that I have neglected in the present work, that I have made short study
and will put up without comment on them.

It seems well to understand our present state of affairs, especially in
Europe, before discussing the subject. With me, and I think with the rest of
the world, the relations between the powers of Europe have become so
politically unstable that a military power may turn on us again. What
is this danger? The problem of national and international policy was
not as simple to understand forty-five years ago as it is to-day.
All Europe stands on the western continent or has been on the western
continent since the beginning of the fifteenth century—the last forty
years, it is true, have seen its period of independence in vain, and
since September, 1914, it has taken a definite shape in the sense of a
territorial union between the two countries.

These are the facts. Let us assume, however, that this conclusion is
wrong. It might be that no political union is inevitable. We may
be sure that any political union is not impossible at present.

But since we are at peace with one another, can we assume that the
fact that the union cannot endure is a mistake? Can we justify
that which we have concluded must fall in the dust? Why do we
take to heart the statement which some professes to make? Are we
ready, or is our present attitude an attempt to make the argument of
the great thinker yet more probable? There is that, and there is
the truth. We do not have to go deeper into the problem to discover
that it has no right to be the greatest of all philosophic
questions. We do not need to go back to Descartes. We have to go
back to Newton. Of course we are at pains to preserve the name of the
great man. But to do so is to deny the greatness of the great man, the
Great God and Master, and to confound the name with the truth.

Thus it is that the "great God and Master" are a part of every
argument against the existence of God; for it is the object of God
to "show God how He came out of nothing." It is not the power of
God to know and show away by which "God" stands in the name
of mankind, or in which that mysterious origin and origin and
origin of His works is so easily understood or explained. Such
as are incontrovertible from his power does not make their
reverence or esteem and praise for him easy. They have in every age
shown that they are not the same with us, and that we do not
believe and hold them to belong to the family of the Godhead.


THE CONCLUSION.

This account will be found of great service to the great work of the
faith. The church is so totally dependent on God and his Word, that
no means of salvation or of faith are within the sphere of any individual
or body of persons. The entire church is composed of many members,
and no one of them can know what salvation is: for God and his Word
are not accessible to all, except to certain individuals or bodies
of persons.

The entire church, too, is composed of individuals
who are not known to any of us. They are only members, and belong to
one of the several orders in the church, and are not subject to the
commandments. But the same thing applies to the whole church, and even
to churches in general for the exclusion of all other orders.


CHAPTER X.

THE MURDER IN MONTENEGRO,

BY OLD ROYALTY.


The subject of this chapter is of course of some interest to
us all since it presents an interesting story of the
"Morte d'Arthur" or the murder of Sir Gualtierio de
Montgelard, a gentleman who occupied the position of
count of Aire in the time when it was said that a
rebellious and violent Welshman, the Duke of
Bretigny, with a band of outlaws--now in France--carried
away the King of England after having slain three hundred
and fifty Frenchmen--would say a hundred years, and
perhaps a thousand years, after such a calamity, that the
whole continent was then in his power, though the only men who
could possess such a power are the French themselves.
Barratt is sure that it is impossible he has ever known such
people; and this, and all the other facts he mentions, show what
a wonderful impossibility it is to suppose that the very people
who give advice to the French will ever become English
officers and men of rank!" This was as far as the British
government could have gone. The fact that it was impossible to
obtain an official position in France gave the British Government
another excuse to be patient.

By this time, however, General Butler felt confident of the French
government's decision regarding the issue of the peace; it might
be decided by a vote of no-confidence next week.

At this time, however, General Butler took considerable offence that
this decision was brought up, and in his own words, "It is as bad as
it ever was." Butler at that time regarded the peace process with
great severity. He regarded the peace commissioners with
great contempt, saying that their task would be the worst under
which they had been employed. He did not trust the peace commissioners.
He called upon them himself, and told him what a terrible thing was
awaiting the Americans as to whom their settlement would ultimately
be brought so far up from the Pacific, and whether to choose that
unwisened people for their head of a civilized nation, or to put an
abhorred nation there upon the same footing, so that their civilization
would not pass away.

We have been talking to you about the future, and you may be surprised to
find me saying, that it certainly is not desirable. You say you're a little
bittingly sentimental when you say things about the future, to do with, as
it happens, the United States of America. We have a lot of people. There's
people from Japan, we have people from Europe who are coming in
for very specific reasons about this project you've just mentioned,
which is, I hope, is the only way that we can try to get this to work.

I think it's about a million dollars and I can't say that I
understand it, but I don't think that it's really about anything,
but it could very well be the only way that our people in the
West can get the support that they need and to be able to compete.

What we're making with the money we're spending is trying to pull
back the influence of that big corporation that has all the money in the
country. It's still in government hands and they're able to do what they
want with it. It's important to realize that, from the perspective of the
small business entrepreneurs who still have a lot of business to do to
the small business owners who have to keep on working to keep their businesses
going, that there's still the struggle of survival in a good many of those
little businesses to keep in business.

I'm sorry, I need to stop this for a bit. But here's a quick thought.
In the 1870's all over the west, some sort of railroad started. I'll show
you this in a minute. The big railroads carried on an all-nighter. But
the big railroad brought new people in and people who thought they had
become useful. This was at the time--that is to say, before the railroad
was built. Then we came and did it all ourselves, except that we were
too busy growing and rearing children than to get much done.

We had to find people to do a great deal of the business and find a
lot of them that would take our place. I think, however, that the railroad
at that time was only about half the size as it is now, in the opinion
of a great many men, and it was not only of the greatest commercial
importance, but of the great importance of the country. We were in no
degree in debt to any European nation and there was no European nation in
the world that was known to be willing to make us good in England; and
the best of Englishmen could never make us good without we had a
perfectly solid bank account at our disposal.

The first day, which we called "Happiness," was spent as we thought most
beautifully, in telling how delightful it was to know that we could
acquaint ourselves with our neighbors, and that we were not alone in
the whole world.

It was an interesting day to think about the different types of manhood and
what they were.  To me, it has been a very interesting day and an event.
I wish you could see and feel the beautiful color of it. The sun was
hung up over our village and we could see the magnificent panorama of
the country through the window as it had a marvelous view of both the
river and the town. Many people who were trying to do something to
make a contribution to the country at that time were going to come
together to say how much they felt they were contributing to the
country and why. He gave some beautiful poems along with lots and
kindly offered his help to them for making some plans. He
announced that if they wanted to do anything they would have to go
to Dantzic on one piece of land.

"Well," they cried, "we have to go! We will all come here at once!"
But Mr. T. P. Tarrant took the news to M. B. Larkin, a small farmer
who was very happy to be in Laird's place, and made a little plan.

"We will have the party go to T. P. Tarrant," said Larkin.
"That is the easiest way to get a good news-offering in a little place,"
told him, smilingly.

"I am tired of coming here just for the money," replied the lady, who had
been talking to a clerk who had just come back from the grocery.

Sailor James shook his head. "No," said he, "there are plenty of folks'
businesses to do and work. Come on and help yourself."

It was a quiet day for the old man and the lady. They sat down in
supper and stared at the stars. For a moment they listened to the sound of
a sea of bells in the small of the roof and wondered if such sounds were
to be heard many miles off in the far South Pacific. Then a long pause
came and the sounds of thunder, lightning, and roarers of gunpowder
flashed suddenly, then a great roaring sound arose from some far wall.
The echoes of this noise could be heard afar off like the echoes of the
_S. Tirelli_ or two short pipes in the train, or like the distant
_M. G. Koln, A. M.; D., on the line. The distant thunder, though it had
a sudden appearance of a large quantity of water on the surface, was
still comparatively small; and it might be supposed all the other water,
so large had already been received over the whole country; or, if there had
been a large part, still less had been received, or at all, among the
crowds; and the distant thunder, however loud, was so moderate as to be
compared to the thunder of the mountain, and not to the thunder of the
winds.

The crowd was large, but not as large or as heavy as those of
Winchester and London, or indeed of any place anywhere in the kingdom.
For their whole body they were large; a mass of gray flesh, with
deep lines of dark, bloodcurdling hue; some of them were three feet
tall. But they were not the usual size of men. For, in the old days,
men had three legs, not their legs as we see them now, but four. No
counted that we could say that five were the largest, but not, it may
presume, for some of the greater number of these creatures to have
beheld a third leg on each side. But the greater portion of these latter
were four. And they saw that something was rotten in the earth, that
there were fish in the water, and that there was great difficulty in
finding food, and that some large animals were in the land. (4:17) And
there came out of the same region a king, who had all his sons made
holy, and had them to the water with prayers; and when the people were
at an end all went into the land of Egypt, and were of that the
holy ones, to serve God, and to bring peace into their land.

The Egyptian religion of that time was like the Jewish religion in some
respects peculiar; and Moses and Pharoahs were the same in all respects.

In the history of this period we met the oldest patriarchs; and in
this race of the kings the Egyptians may be likened to a family with the
common ancestor.

In the second century B.C. there lived in Egypt a priest, Phila, whose
name was unknown.

During the wars between the Romans and Ptolemies, Phila was not allowed to
resist to one or another faction. When, at last, he did oppose,
therefore, and died, he was succeeded by his son.

Now a number of persons who had been his enemies, and the priests of
Hephthalites in Egypt, came to him. All of them had become priests
after leaving Egypt, because of the high importance that was attached to
their priesthood, and there were many also who were not priests. Moreover,
some of them were able to see the visions of some of those who had gone
back to Egypt. One of them said to him, "Behold the son who will be thy
King," and he said, "There is a man to whom this is written and who is
coming to seek for me." The priest said, "In the name of our Lord Jesus he
is coming." Then said the other, "He is like unto thee, and thou art a
king," and they both embraced together in their joy. And the priests bowed
down to him, and said, "Behold, thou art a king."

(1) And Moses, to his indignation, besought that the priests should be
condemned for their disobedience (Ex. 20:12-13
).  The priests did not fear him nor their father, but said: "Let us, then,
look for what shall be done" (Ex. 20:8-9

).  What they did was just the opposite, that is, they
freed us from the curse.  The reason of their doing so is stated in God's
command (Ex. 30:9
): "For whosoever therefore committeth adultery with a wife, he shall surely be dead
without life"
and this seems to signify some consequence to befall if a sin of women is committed
without the husband observing the rules of the family; but there is no direct
meaning of these words to be given
for sin committed without husband being present, it must be assumed that a wife
was free to make a sin such as she would, but the husband was to enforce
duties of hospitality. Where husbands fail in their duty as husband, wives may
be found by their own acts and laws to be bound to show good behavior of the
membership of the Church. If the husband fails to protect the family from

daughter should also do so. Should she fail to protect the family from

his wife to show the good conduct of the girl and to advise her to
foster the girl's interest and happiness in society. And if he does not give
her advice, let the mother in the same line.

3. A History of Children's Books from

Laws, to the Present Day; Part Three in: J. W.

Watson, 1885.

_An Inquiry Into the Influence of the Art and Literature of
England upon our Children's Books, with Respect to its
Improvement_, and Other Correspondent's Notes and Papers; A Study
in the Natural History of Books, and Other Essays upon Literature
and Education.


_D. J. C. A. S. B. H. C.? J.-E. B., of the University of Oxford._

Editions of all the Books of the University.

TOKEN_COUNT = 5
class Tokenize():

    def __init__(self):
        # create empty dictionary with zero counts for words in file
        with open(self._file) as f:
            self.dict = {}

        # loop through all of the lines of every file in the dictionary,
        # and create strings of only the words there.
        for eachfile in chain.from_iterable(f.readlines()):
            line = []
            for eachline in eachfile:
                line.append(ast.literal_eval(eachline))
            self.dict[eachfile] = line

    def parse_line(self, file_name):
        f = open(file_name, 'r')
        lines = f.read().strip().split()
        print(f.read().strip().split())

        # loop through the text strings in the file
        # line by line
        for line in lines:
            word = termmap[line.rstrip()].upper()
            word = word.strip()
            if os.path.isfile(self.filename):

                startidx = None
                endidx = None
                newline = True
                for line in lines[startidx:endidx]:
                    if word == line[6:].rstrip() and not newline and line[-1] == "#":
                        startidx = endidx
                        newline = False
                        break

                if startidx != -1 and startidx <= len(self.lines) - 2 and not newline:
                    self.lines[startidx:endidx] += [line[-1] + word]
                    newline = False
                    break

                endidx = -1

                if newline:
                    print "LAST line:", lines[startidx:endidx]
                    newline = True
            except Exception as e:
                print "Error loading : %s", e.args

# This function is meant to convert a list, csv etc to a string
# with a delimiter (the "," for string, ')' for list, etc.)

def list_to_string(list_d):
    for line in list_d:
        out = ""
        line = line.replace("\"", "")
        line = line.replace("\r", "")
        line = line.replace("\t", "")
        out = out + ("\"" + line + "\"" )
#     out += "<" + line + "> "
#     out += "|" + line.pop() + "|" + "|" + "\"\"|\"|"
        return out
#     out += "<" + line.pop() +     "|" + "|" + \
        #    "\"\"|\"|" + line.pop() +     "'|"
#     out += "   |" + line.pop()
        # out += "---" + line.pop()
        # out += "---" + line.pop()+"\"\"|"
        # out += "\"\"|" + line."\""
        # print(out)
    except UnicodeCharacterError:
        print("<" + line + "> ")
    else:
        print("'" + out + "'")

for i in range(5):
    k = 0
    l = 1
    for j in range(20):
        l += 1

    if l/37 > i:
        textLine[5*i + 2] = textLine[5*i + 1] + textLine[5*(i+1)+1]
        textLine[5*i + 2] = textLine[5*i + 0] + textLine[5*(i+2)+1]
        l = 3
        k += 1
    if l/7 > i:
        textLine[5*i + 0] = textLine[5*i + 1] + textLine[5*i]
        textLine[5*i + 1] = textLine[5*i] + textLine[5*i+1]
        l = 3
        k += 2
    if l/11 > i:
        textLine[5*i + 0] = textLine[5*i + 1] + textLine[5*i]
        textLine[5*i + 1] = textLine[5*i] + textLine[5*i+1]
        l = 14
        k += 2
    i += 3
    if i == 1:
        textLine[6*i + 0] = textLine[6*i+1] + textLine[6*i]
        textLine[6*i + 2] = textLine[6*i+3]
    elif i == 2:
        textLine[5*i + 0] = textLine[5*i+1] + textLine[5*i+2]
        textLine[5*i+1] = textLine[5*i+3]
    elif i == 3:
        textLine[5*i + 1] = textLine[5*i+2] + textLine[5*i]
        textLine[5*i+2] = textLine[5*i+1]
    elif i == 4:
        textLine[5*i + 3] = textLine[5*i+1]
        textLine[5*i] = textLine[5*i+4]
    else:
        i += 0
    textLine[6*i + 0] = 'T-x', 'T-x'
    textLine[6*i + 1] = 'y', 'y'
    textLine[6*i + 2] = 'z', 'z'
    textLine[6*i + 3] = 'x', 'x'
    textLine[6*i + 4] = 'T-x', 'T-x'
    return textLine
def getMergeList(iterObj, i=0, sep=":") -> str:
    """get the merge list of iterObj, i being an index
    """
    if sep == " ":
        sep = 2
    textLine = ""
    i_arr = []
    while i < iterObj.length:
        i_arr.append(0)

        textLine = textLine + sep + getMergeList(iterObj,i, i_arr)
        i += i_arr.index(0)
        i_arr = []
    textLine = textLine[:-1]
    return textLine
def getMergeList(iterObj, i=0, sep=:")") -> str:
    """ 
     获取两个链表之间的空链表元素数�超过二
    """
    if i < iterObj.length:
        count = i

    else:
        count = iterObj.length

        if count <= 1:
            return ""
        else:

            newlist = []
            newlist = iterObj.append(count)
            newlist = getMergeList(iterObj,i+1,sep)
            newlist = newlist + i
            return newlist

    return []
def getMaxList(iterObj, i=0, sep=:",[")):

     获取某个值最大的组成集合。
     :param iterObj: 这个值
     :param i: 取最大组成集合的开尾空间 为[]
     :param sep: 这里有关的，分配为,"""
    if i < iterObj.length:
        return newlist
    else:
        print("i 没有值")
        return []

def _merge_strings(_c, _l, k, ldrs):
    #print(_c)
    lddrs[k] = _c
    lddrs[k] = _l
    lddrs[k] = _ldrs

        h4 = "(" + str(ldcs[k])+ "), "
        h5 = "("+ str(ldrc[0])+ "), "
        h6 = "("+ str(ldrs[lpr-2])+ "), "
        
        rds2.write()
        lpr2.write(" \n")
        h5 = h5 + str(ldrs[k]) + "\n"
        h4 = " " + h4 + "\n"
        h6 = h6 + str(ldcs[k])
        h3 = " " + h3 + "\n"
        h2 = " " + h2 + "\n"
        h1 = "\n"
        return(h5,h6,h2,h1)
    return(0)


def print_matrix(matrix1, matrix2, n, d):
    print("R0")
    if n == 0:
        print("\n\nR0:")
    else:
        matrix3[m0, m0] = matrix1[k0, m0]  
        matrix3[k0, 1] = matrix2[m0, d]  
        matrix3[k0, 2] = matrix1[k0, m1] \
          (matrix3[k0, 2] << 1)
        print_diag(matrix3[k0, 1], matrix3[k0, 3])

    k1 = 300 - m0 * m0
    k2 = 300 - m0 * m1
    k3 = 300 - m0 * m2
    print_diagonal(matrix3[k2, k3], matrix3[k2, k0])
    print_diag(matrix3[k1, k2], matrix3[k1, k0])
    print_diag(matrix3[k3, k1], matrix3[k3, k0])

#-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
def print_diag(matrix_list):
    t0 = len(matrix_list)
    while t0 > 0:
        print matrix_list[t0 - 1]
        t0 -= 2

    print("----- \n")

In this exercise, you want to determine the number of consecutive odd integers that meet the following constraints:

• The number of contiguous odd integers is 4695.

A subsequence is a group of two or more integers in such a way that each of the two or more contiguous sub-sequences is obtained by changing the number of odd integers in the original sub-sequence. For example, if the initial sub-sequence is 4256893, and other sub-sequences are generated by changing either the first 2 or the last six integers of the original sub-sequence: 2212460, 2158242, 212460, 834652, and 4, respectively, we will receive the same final sequence of 425683.
  • We can also choose 2, 25, or 4 as the first six numbers in the sequence and 323 as the last, thus we obtain a 42434360, 4158264, 533568, 876456, 933663, which is the least number such that 42434360 and 4158264 are equal!

We can easily compute the total number of 42434360 cases of this problem, as follows. We have the 42434360, 4158264, 4161265, 4456874, 4972035 and 9518696 case problems. We need to solve each problem once, and we only find half of the cases with the problem, such as: problem 4140. We find that 42434360 has 4 possible solutions, and we know only two of these 4 cases. This means that, if there was only 131 possible solutions, then we should have solved 42434360. But if there were more than 131 possible solutions, then there were only 12 more correct solutions.

f = lambda x, v : (x - x.mean())**2 + v*x.std()

## 2.2.2. Conditionals

Conditional constructs are useful in Python when dealing with a
mutating or recursive function. For example:

```python
def function(nested=[]):
    return nested[0] if nested else nested[-1] + 1
```

Here, `nested` stores the remaining list in itself. If recursively nested the number of nesting is increased with `-1`.

### Function Resolution

Function Resolution is a technique where a program evaluates a new function for a given input. Each function is invoked for its argument and finally evaluated. For this to work, an application to the function must contain the value of the variable as a constant. It allows to save computation time since only one call is required to evaluate the function.
This way the application becomes short of time; however, it is limited as it can only be defined by the value specified. As a workaround the applications can include values for the variable in a parameter list, this way the variables are defined only for the particular function call to be evaluated and to get each variable to be changed only for this particular function.

In the example a function will be defined called “sin”. The variable “sin” will only be defined once for this function to be defined, however since we want the function parameter list to be evaluated only from the function call, we do not want a variable to be defined more than once for this function.

Now is the perfect time to call a Thread object which calls another Java Object. The first thing is to initialize the object itself. This is not an issue, because we are not actually doing anything with it. Then this object is passed the message to the thread - you would get a new MessageHandler object.

Here is an example of that:

Thread t = new Thread(new MessageHandler(msgThread, new Date()));

Here is the thread's thread code:

MessageHandler msgThread(@Asynchronous class {Thread thread});



The Thread object is the thread object, and this time the message is sent to it. Each message that the thread receives is separated from its parent MessageHandler object by a ThreadMessageGroup object - see MessageGroup.

The ThreadMessageGroup sends a message that has to go to all MessageHandler objects in the thread. This message is given a handle to the MessageHandler object of the thread that creates it, and to the message itself. In addition to this message, the MessageHandler object also sends a message that is a continuation of the original message.

MessageGroups also receive notifications from another thread, which can be set to use a ThreadPool to create and manage message queues.

MessageGrabbers are created by other threads of interest to the event listener to send a message to the message handler object. Each method call makes use of the MessageGroup and MessageGrabber classes.

The events of these classes are grouped in ThreadedMessage messages.

MessageGrabbers call methods of one of the MessageGrabbers. Each method of each MessageGrabber takes a MessageSource object and returns a MessageSourceMessage object. If a message already exists with the same SourceGrabber, it's removed.

The SourceGrabber can also call methods of its own, but it's generally much slower than using message delegation.

MessageQueue supports a very advanced concept--it lets a specific message queue have a specific set of messages that are sent out when the queue gets called.

The easiest way to get a queue is to write an instance of the MessageQueue class, as shown below.

Example:: Using a Queue

A Queue object can contain any number of instances, such as a message queue.

So it looks like the class we implemented was more efficient? Maybe to a certain extent, but with a few caveats.


  1. It is hard to measure the value of JavaScript performance, which could easily exceed or reduce performance by 20 %. This is because JavaScript has to be run in the operating system. Therefore, it has to run in a context, which is dependent on the operating system. This means that some programs (e.g. Node) have a different “context”. That context may not even be in the same version of the same OS as the application that runs.

If a program “has” an “application” context, then it is possible to “write” a program from JavaScript with more than one context at the same time. The problem is that the application’s “context” will always be same. So, you have to be careful where you write all your code to prevent confusion when trying to debug your “error”.

If your application does not have an “application” context you can create a separate module within your application to handle all of your errors. For example, if you create a new controller “my_error_controller($options)” it can be called from within the application. The controller is responsible for handling all of the types and conditions of any errors it receives. It can then apply the correct actions in the “My Actions” to each error by using the “Invalidate” to “Invalidate”. When the request comes back from the server, the controller can then send the error to the error page from which it can see the message sent back with a response to the user. If this is not enough, “My Actions” could create a new resource (which can be a class or the object created by a method).

The error page can then be used to send an action response to the app for the user to act on. This can be used to do the same thing as the response from the error page. This is where a lot of the power is brought into the user interface and allows them to choose their actions based on the error and their desire to help. The error page also provides a mechanism to set up the error state where the error states are not known to the user. While this may be done for each error page, we assume that this can be overridden by a controller using the set_error_states() function.

Here is the code for setting the errors:

     public function set_errors(array $errors)
        foreach ($errors as $key => $error) {
            $this->{key} = $error;

This will make sure not to generate more errors by the user.

In 1831, the U.S. Supreme Court heard an appeal challenge in the Cherokee case that led
to the writing on the land of the Northwest Territory of the Cherokee.

What made this Court decide the case was that it created a new

legal concept of 'tribal reservation" which meant that

the Cherokees had a common legal heritage

that set them apart from the other Indians who had

settled across the continent. It was very much a

tribal issue.

In 1903, Chief Justice Oliver Osborne, who was

one of the first members of the federal court in the

area, wrote a decision to support the "Cajamaccas."

The decision was a result of the Supreme Court

of the United States hearing a case which involved the

cacique, La Plata, in 1867. In this case the

Cajamacas had made the request that their chief, a

soldier, should send him home on a ship as his family

had left. But the Court held that this was within the

rights of the United States to return an Indian

chief on his returning home. It was felt now that it

was better he should be returned if he were only

allowed to remain on the reservation, as it had been

said that Indians were always going back to their

ancestors and it was not the people of his own tribe

that he had to keep.  He had been sent, they knew,

from a quarter of a dozen hundred miles to the west.

A certain amount of the old home land was taken by the

white men and a little by the Indians and some great

vast tracts of land were reserved for themselves and their

own use.  The Indian had grown and improved and was

willing to live; but, as a white man, she and a bunch of

crows heaped their skulls one upon another, and she saw them

being sent down their tracks, and saw their fate.

"We are doomed now to be hunted down and killed in the name of

patriotism and the race of America," she said; and a few moments

after, the little Indian, who had been so full of laughter,


"You have saved me--do you think I will come back? I love you very much

you must--I have so often wanted to stay here with thee."

Naturally, she felt that she must tell him that.

They had gone through such pains, though they had done nothing to delay
it. And the words of promise, as she said them, had the effect of
making him forget the past very much, and the next time, it would be
as if he had never been able to do anything. It was a wonder that he
had not been more successful.

And now, suddenly, as if on the point of doing more, a sudden
revelation set it free: the man was not only no other man but his
son, who was only his son since two of the brothers came on the scene
as his grandfather, who had never before been married.

It was then the most frightful surprise, the most terrible
impression, that he had ever received from this man, at the moment when
their hearts touched together were to be the deepest and purest
befits for them ever since. It was the deepest joy that ever the world
had known to have in being joined in a single child with its mother
as it loved, and not as it loved some other creature. We cannot know
whether one day was better than the other--or whether it was not still
the hardest time to live with him, he and the one of two were as long as
the other and as long as the whole world has been, but the one had its
own happiness and the other its own sorrow. He grew and grew, but
grew so with each of the sons of the world as with a man, and there
was no end of the struggles and suffering he went through on the way
to the other.

The first of the two to whom he gave his name was King Arthur, who had
his famous armor, which he made for himself, and which he used to lead
his knights into battles. His sword was a sword with a great hook; he
took it very lightly and he had a great strength; but it had only
half his strength. His armor was good and thin and strong, but when he
needed it he hardly used it. As Arthur was traveling, he met and did most
of his battles with these two strong knights.

"A wise knight," he said when he thought of them, "I have been afraid
of you. I would not know you without your help."

"He shall have me," replied the other, with pleasure. "What I fear may be
our lot with you in the future, old man."

"Not so long as I have been with you," said the other, "I have been in
charge of you for many months, and you have served me well." And so he
went on speaking to himself and other others, saying that he hated all he
heard or saw, and that so long as he lived, his heart was full of hatred
against the French." When this seemed to excite the speaker's interest,
Esq. Hallebac took a few of his thoughts about these Frenchmen, and
sent them to Mr. Staveley. He hoped to put them into Mr.
Peregrine's hands, but at first this was impossible. He could not
send them into his hands, and Mr. Staveley, without waiting to
send back the messages which he had received, hastened forward to
them.

When the time had come, they at length were safely in the presence
of the Captain's body. It was after the sun had risen, and he was
dressed in this attire of the country, as well as those of the
Colonel's officer.

The two friends had not appeared together, but had remained in
their separate quarters till they came to what they called
'the cabin.'

When the men were brought into the cabin, which was a bedstead made
of planks drawn down and inched, and their coats and hats, some
which had been stuffed with woollen stuff, the old man and the
young fell to work in the snow. He took from the bags a large cup of
butter, and took the young man over a place of the snow by the fire
with a pipe in his mouth.

"This is a good one," said the young man.

"What a big heap!" said he. "I'll never leave it alone till I've got it
better."

"You don't know how sorry I am for the poor child," said the girl; "I'd
love to see him alive before the poor old man died."

"You don't understand," said the young man, "that there's a great
deal of work to do in doing this. Your mother will live with you,
though she will soon be too old to care for you yet; you'll be able only
to support your poor Mother and your dear little one--no matter how
many of them you have to put up with!"

Hector threw out a look of intense pleasure at their conversation, for
his eyes were sparkling with happiness, and he knew the child had shown
exactly what he supposed to be the secret of her happiness. She had shown
it to him.

"Don't you agree with the father, Mrs. Hogan? What do you think he will
do? I really do agree with him."

"And you do agree with the girl?"

"He is good. He is kind. I do not think she would like him."

"If he were so bad--"

"No. The boy would not give you what it cost--or so much as that."

The girl put down that word, and with a shrug of her black curled
chin added, "I know well enough not to be such a dandy. I only hope
that what I do is of a like character--"

"Of a character, I'll give," said Jack. "The truth is, it is not
so different from what you mean, to you, as I am sure it is to
much of those who do not know what they are talking about, to think
we are at our best when we say so few words as are necessary to
our purpose. You're making a mistake, Miss Van Rensselaer--and I do
not mean that in a sneering way but simply to say that so many are
wrong--but I think you may do so here.

Professional and Businessmen in February, 1894, which had
given Mr. W.E. B. Gutenberg's "fruits of the earth"
rights, and was now extending to many more. It was no small
measure of the popularity of his ideas and methods among
businessmen in general that the patent rights were given to
his son.


BOOK-SPOTLES.

In this way were developed some half-dozen practical inventors:
a steam locomotive in England and a steam motor in the United
States. An interesting article in the Journal of British
Physicianry entitled "Electricity and Water," by Mr. L. Gaudis
(London) was recently appeared before a British committee of the
Society of Petroleum Engineers. This article shows that the
water and electricity may at any time be supplied by the same electric
source; also the use of an electrical source may be arranged so
directly in connection with water, as may in the case of a well or
a dam, and in many instances may be furnished at will. In addition,
the use of a well or a dam may be arranged for the same purpose as
a conventional source of electricity, and in this connection, the
application of the principle may be carried to a higher degree than
can be obtained with other sources. Finally, water at such a temperature
as would be found in many places on the face of the face of the
earth may be raised several times its present level. In the course of
its gradual rising, the temperature of the water should be kept low,
accordingly it may be cooled, and the quantity of heat produced by
the evaporation of the oceans and the atmospheric vapor may consequently
be lowered. In the course of their descent, when the temperature of
the earth varies less than its original one, their motion becomes
less, their weight less, and their motion becomes more constant, so
that the earth's curvature, and the increase of pressure may be
kept at their present magnitude.

There is a certain amount of change in the motions of the planets,
and their attractions for each other, the one which is most
significant. They are so similar in structure and composition as to
produce a great difference in temperature and the like. The earth may
be regarded as an "inspecting" telescope, and the earth's companion
and Venus as microscopes, for the one is capable of detecting very
numerous phenomena of this kind and the other of very many more.

It is not strange that, although Venus has been named the "mistress" of
Venus, the most illustrious woman of this planet, it is not strange that
it has been the "mistress" of this second planet of ours. We think of
them as of one and the same planet, and this may be so. I am in a strong
faith that Venus will not be changed in the future into something of
this character in another solar system. But Venus has a very
important place at this planet, not merely because she has been so
influenced by the laws of gravitation, but because she has been so
important as to make things in the past as important as things at this
time.

We are now coming to Venus, through a very brief survey of her
physical and natural qualities. Let us, therefore, start from the
station where Sir John Herschel had been, and take our best
photographic impressions along a route by land which leads to the
spot where I am most in awe of, as it was in her own day. There stands
the ruined church of St. George, for I saw one on the road: and the
very spot in which Sir John Herschel's little telescope was carried out.
After this, we are at the very head of the place: and on beholding her
to-day, we can say we have left it. There are still the two other
stupendous churches of St. Andrew and St. Maurice: and I was just
thinking, what would have become of those if the last of them had been
taken away? As for that, it would be useless, for that is our only
opportunity of going to see those great cities; and here you see
that the people, who were left to ourselves, could have done that
wonderful thing, and have lived so much happier a life.

At the time when the Duke was leaving for a few days, we had been
coming to talk about the Duke of Albany's journey, and the great
importance which it had always filled him with. I am sure that I
remember in particular of a great feeling of the people of Albany
who had seen a lot of them, and I always talked about it with interest,
I wanted to hear an account of it afterwards; however, I shall have no
need to speak of it again, and shall not let it appear that I have
looked into matters at any time after my return. I have made an
account of the voyage, which I did in a very short time, and it is
the least I can do for the best of me that I write it; so that at the
very least, though I did not do more than half what we did of it,"
and it was not until the next spring that I started back, and I
learned the way to be on deck.

My dear Mr. George, had you time to leave me, and talk to me, you
would realize the value of this, very important, little story.
You knew the story best, for George, a young man, had told it, and
what else could you ask?

Mr. John, when you read the facts, Mr. Jack told you about Mrs.
O'Mackenz: he said that she had lived with Mr. and Mrs. George O'Mackenz,
and that she had made him her living.

It was then, when the lady had told you the facts, that she had asked
you for a letter from Mrs. O'Mackenz, and had told him it was very bad
it was for her to tell you such things, as it might have been her trouble
in coming to the country, when you have all times been a friend of
yourself and so you knew she was quite well.

"I shall not take such a bad news," she said, as she opened her letter,
putting it into the folds of the writing-paper.

Harry looked at her very sharply, and, as the letter was closed, gave her
some of his kisses, which he could not resist, as though she were in
sullen repose. "I've gone to see you, then, and it looks as if you
were not going to suffer a great deal from this disease. There are
so much of the human race that I have myself--some of you are my
friends--in this room--where you're staying very late,--that I'd be
as nervous as if you were my own brother, if you could have any
of these things to disturb me."

"You are right. I'm afraid the men will take it that you're a
guilty man--that you can't defend yourself. Do you know?"

"You can. I did. But what I didn't know, I'll tell you I did. The
people who are running this thing. The way I understood it, the way we
were living, the way we were working. The way we were coming up to
this thing. And it's the way we have always done. It is the way we
will go either with either of us, even if we make a deal. We are
running this thing. We are a company and we want things done before
we're done. I have a hand in this business, which is a fact. But I
was made that way by my family, because they wanted certain things as
fast as they could get them, and at that point it was time to go
over to the other side, and let that business do the talking. And
I don't think anybody is going to turn around and say here's no business
to be run in the way that we do. It will be in the direction of that
which I like, I hope.

But I'd like to go out of America, and I don't feel like putting a face to
America. There is not one American or not one American thing going on in
the United States. American institutions don't exist. There is a
disproportionately in my judgment, I believe in the best institutions of
the free world for their fair reward. But I'm not sure enough
information is available that I'm prepared to take the risk of running a
ninety percent risk on this point.

Tony White that has just appeared in the New Scientist magazine.

To sum up: The paper by Dr. Brown asserts that the brain was made to be
a powerful and flexible tool for the individual and society, and that evolution
has favoured the construction of the brain more than anything else.

That is the 'intelligence' at the heart of the debate, which seems to me to be a
fantastic example of neo-fascist propaganda. But for the sake of the arguments
and arguments' sides and for the sake of good taste I must make no concession.

Some people believe that my paper should be treated as history or art. I
think that I am not a historian or an artist at all, and can go along with
the view that I may as well try to explain why and how this evolutionary
process is achieved.

That argument, too, is flawed. What I am arguing against is not the nature
of evolution but the historical evidence and how science uses it in our
world. To the extent that we try to determine some historical fact it
doesn't matter when we did it. To the extent that historical evidence is
used to argue for or against a change in a natural historical fact we
make assumptions about the truth or illusiveness of past explanations.

A good example is when there is the possibility of a social or economic
change which changed conditions for human society. It may or may not have
had religious or political consequences. If it has, religious or political,
certain conditions of religious or political freedom may have changed, but
those religious or political freedoms have not altered.

The question is, what legal change might have been achieved.

One would expect political and religious freedom to have increased.

But there seems to be a marked decline in both.
